I think in terms of non-interactive euros, I appreciate this one, even if it does fall out as a puzzle plus a jockeying game for turn order.

RATING: 9.1
I love this game. I have only played at the two-player count. I really like the (very controversial) double-moves that are possible in two-player games. We use two house rules to tighten up the experience for two players: 1. replace the Viziers with Artisans, and 2. play on a slightly smaller grid of 6 x 4 tiles. I adjust down the number of meeples and camels accordingly.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
[I]tl;dr - Five Tribes wraps a really interesting game around mancala, and the fact that you WILL be leaving good stuff behind for other players to pick up. Don't play with AP-prone people, but this is a great hour-long game if you like tactical games.[/I]
[COLOR=#CCCCFF]____________________________[/COLOR]

Five Tribes is best described by its uniqueness. You use a reverse worker placement concept, where you are picking workers off of existing spaces, then moving them elsewhere on the board to conduct an action and use the meeple's abilities. Meeples can buy you goods, help you court the favor of powerful genies, assassinate other meeples, and earn you victory points towards the game end. 

Five Tribes is an incredibly tactical game. Every turn, you will be scoping out the game board for the best possible turn, and then bidding on the turn order (which brings in some nice bluffing and planning -- it's possible to entirely overbid as happened to one player in the latest game we played). Because of this, it can be prone to analysis paralysis, but this doesn't happen often if you keep players moving. 

I love Five Tribes because it presents so many difficult decisions as well as so many paths to victory. I sprinted to a tight win in our latest game because I specialized on the market almost to the exclusion of everything else. I've seen other players win with Djinns, with conservative bidding, and with great area control on the board. The great part is, you need to focus on multiple victory point engines in addition to your primary one, because one specialty won't be enough to bring you to victory. 

If you love tactical games, beautiful artwork, and a great, elegant game that plays in under an hour and a half, Five Tribes is for you. I couldn't think of another game that better uses worker placement in such an innovative fashion, and it's one of my go-tos when we want a medium-heavy game to play. Highly recommended.

[COLOR=#FFFFFF][BGCOLOR=#66CC33][b] EXPANSION(s) OWNED: [/b][/BGCOLOR][/COLOR]
[COLOR=#CCCCFF]____________________________[/COLOR]

[thing=176677][/thing] [b][BGCOLOR=#00CC00][COLOR=#00cc00].[/COLOR]10[COLOR=#00cc00].[/COLOR][/BGCOLOR][/b] - the best way to expand a game. New mechanics are introduced, but they aren't ridiculously overpowered and don't add needless complexity. With the exception of teaching newbies, I think I'll always play with the expansion since it feels so . . . natural. It adds a bigger game board, with a few obstacles (mountains and the void), and the Artisans are a great sixth tribe. Adding the magical items to the base game works out well as well, and it can really be a viable path to victory.

RATING: 8
2 to 4 Players
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 10
When I first played Five Tribes, I loved it but worried about analysis paralysis. However, I love this game more every single time I play it and it is now one of my favorite games, even if it's not one that gets to the table constantly. The beauty of this game is that it is relatively simple mechanically (the only real mechanic in the game is the mancala, so once you understand it, it's just a matter of what the different tribes do and what djinns and market items are available). Despite this simplicity, this game is full of strategic and tactical depth. You are both required to plan long-term to create a cohesive strategy and you must be tactical and respond to opportunities that arise (and avoid creating opportunities). While long thinking times can be an issue, this game plays very well once everyone is familiar with the game. Familiarity tends to shorten the thinking considerably and players who are used to the game are more comfortable with waiting for a while for players to put together their move. As a result, this game is perfect for when you want to play a heavier game but also want to socialize with those around the table. As long as everyone sets a personal mental timer for themselves, giving themselves only a limited amount of time to find the best move, the game plays very well. Strangely enough, exactly because the options for moves are often so unlimited, players are more willing to let go and make a 'good' move that gets them plenty of points without spending an eternity seeking out the 'best' move. Perfection.

RATING: 7
4 plays / 2,3 players

LIKE: Great production, tangible game bits. Many good choices, almost too many, but some minor strategies can be found. I enjoyed the base point scoring, modular boards, and djinn powers.
DISLIKE: Board changes between every turn which induces analysis paralysis. If a player doesn't think about setting other players up, and only focuses on their own point goals, the game can go by quicker. Oh, and there is no need for the slave cards, as the theme is thin. Use the fakirs.

OVERALL: Although my wife didn't care for it, too many rules for her, I was able to teach it quickly to others. Some people find the game too much of a puzzle, but I think that is part of the fun. Not a game for over-analyzers, but a timer can help.

RATING: 7
Five Tribes has very interesting mechanics for action selection. I found it rather refreshing. The downside is that every turn players have hundreds if not thousands of possible moves. You can imagine how a game will turn out if you played with someone prone to indecisiveness or with a will to analyse the situation completely before making any decisions. Good quality components and pleasant graphics.

The gameplay is more of seizing the opportunities rather than of building your position. However, there is some room for long term strategies and cunning which will definitely delight more experienced boardgamers.

RATING: 10
My favorite Bruno Cathala game so far. Five Tribes is a mechanism cocktail but most notably the area control and mancala mechanics are the big gears that move the game. Yes there is card drafting set collection djinns with triggering powers etc etc but those are all added meat on a solid game design that make a good game better. I really enjoy this game. You spend most of it dropping five different meeples mancala style on each module of the board. The last drop triggers one of five different possible powers and possibly gets you ownership of the module, you obtain land, djinns and special sets of cards to gain victory points and win the game. As play progresses and available moves diminish ap can creep in to the game and slow it down but this is a minor issue. The art is also gorgeous. I am jot surprised that the game is getting so much recognition and accolades. Also, if feeding old people and slaves to djinns to make them use powers isn't your thing, the BGG store is currently selling substitute cards to swap out the slaves for.

RATING: 7
A good, solid euro.  The mechanics are pretty straightforward but the strategy is deep enough to keep me engaged.  I like the variety of choices and how everything pushes and pulls on the rest.  Nicely done.

Of course this is all balanced against the inherent paralysis of all those choices and how advance planning is almost entirely fruitless (one player's move can totally destroy and/or change your options).  I can't fault others for delays when it's their turn because I end up the same way - reanalyzing and reassessing.  The dynamic nature of the game is its downfall.

I will play this from time to time, but a 4p game has the potential for serious delay.

FAVOURITE GAME - this game reinvents worker placement and meeples: the game begins with the meeples already in place – and players must cleverly maneuver them over the villages, markets, oases, and sacred places tiles that make up Naqala. How, when, and where you displace these Five Tribes of Assassins, Elders, Builders, Merchants, and Viziers determine your victory or failure. It is an enchanting game, but the planning phase can take up a lot of time.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
[b]18.09.2014 - after 1 play[/b]
Five Tribes is a "heavier" game from Days of Wonder, compared to the usual "TTR"-ian difficulty of their previous games. 

Graphics-wise, it doesn't look too spectacular. I might say that the background of the tiles is too samey and too brownish (yes, I know the setting is a desert one). On the other hand, the Djinns have unique and gorgeous designs. The components (other than the squares that form the modular board) are solid and nice to the touch. 

Theme is completely pasted-on. This game could have been about dinosaurs or SF explorers, still would have played the same. 

Five Tribes has the not-so-used "mancala" mechanism (I don't know any other modern game, except Trajan, that uses it) and this is the core element of the gameplay. The other aspect of the game, the set collection one, can deliver many points at the end of the game, especially by card sets. Also the biding for turn order feels ok. In our game, the Djinn cards were almost never used so I don't know if they are useful.

Plus: the "still" fresh mancala system and the components.
Minus: the AP that the mancala and the biding bring to the game. Because of the ever changing board status, you cannot plan for a move, unless you are determined to bid for first player (at least in the last half of the game). Also, some of the players may leave opportunities for the ones following them, but this is partially handled through the biding every turn.

Although there are more minuses than pluses, I enjoyed the game and would love to play it more, so that I can form a FINAL opinion.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 3
A pet peeve of mine is games in which you can't plan.

Welcome to my own version of hell.

Every turn, after each player takes his or her turn, you are faced with a new board. There are random elements that are not significant but can influence the game play, and the game may simply disallow certain options by virtue of the random nature of the board.

If you are good at (or enjoy) managing random turns of events, this game will appeal to you. If herding cats sounds like a thankless task, well, move along then.

RATING: 9.5
I think this is a 9.5 because I am always desperate to play it. I find it fairly hard to get to the table, but enjoy it a lot every time.  Great game with 3-4 players!

Two negatives:
1) this can get super thinky for some people and they might take forever on their turn.  Those people MAY be mostly me.
2) I feel it is a bad 2 player game.  The two player rules create MANY scenarios where you go twice in a row.  That is not only super powerful, but also exponentially increases the difficulty of optimizing the first of those two moves. It also makes the bidding much harder to calculate.  When bidding who wins the game often hinges on who can calculate the value of taking two turns in a row on the current board state.  That is a VERY hard mental calculation to do well on.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 8.5
OK, this one is entertaining and good-looking to boot. However, since this is a typical Euro, the theme is painfully pasted-on. Also, rules are totally arbitrary and don't follow any sort of thematic logic (you control tiles by emptying them?): this could have been a game about mice in a maze, collecting different sorts of cheese, or whatever. As for the look and the components, everything is gorgeous and nicely made, but I am a bit puzzled by the clash in the illustrations for the Djinns, which don't fit the rest of the game's look. My last rant is that setup is a bit complicated, and the game is definitely on the long side (it could be called "The Djinns of Analysis-Paralysis"). All that being said, all my criticism does not matter: this is a very good game, well-designed and fun to play, and I highly recommend it to everyone.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
Includes:
[thing=176234][/thing]

:arrowE: [u]Synopsis[/u]:
Pick up all meeples on a tile, then drop them one by one, [thing=2448][/thing]-style, on all tiles leading up to your intended destination. The color of the meeple which ends the chain activates all other like-colored meeples on said tile, allowing for a specific action. The available action on the final tile is also activated. Repeat until all meeples are used and no legal moves are left. This is worker placement of the strangest order. Set collection and points-as-money bidding makes for very interesting interactions with the board state, which is very tactical. Buy resources, invoke Djinns, assassinate Viziers, rack in the big points with Builders... there are only a few options, but they all mingle together to allow for a ton of possibilities. Like Queen's Architect, this simply can't be played as a solely-strategic game.

:arrowE: [u]What I like most[/u]:
The combo-tastic potential for this game is excellent, primarily in the two-player game which features a built-in variant allowing for two turns per round. It's possible to chain four consecutive turns back to back, which is a bit dizzying in complexity, as you can line up meeples this turn for bigger payouts next turn. Getting to trigger your Djinn's ability to boost your tile ability which piggy-backs off of your previous meeple ability. Mmm mmm mmm mmm MMM! (I love combos, if you can't tell). 

:arrowE: [u]What I like least[/u]:
I do not find the tiles to be visually distracting, but I do think there are just enough colors represented by the meeples that it can be difficult to identify possible action chains given how much there is to process at a glance. The board state changes so much after each player's turn that it's difficult to plan too far ahead.

:arrowE: [size=13][url=https://play.spotify.com/user/jwilliamparkin/playlist/1HmPnzhnFWjAHkqmUUebp9][b]PLAYLIST[/b][/url][/size]

RATING: 7
This is a pleasant euro driven by a meeple "mancala movement" mechanic.  As a euro, it capably includes, card set collection using green meeples, basic spatial considerations with blue meeples, temples and palm trees, "take that" meeple elimination with red meeples, special "engine" upgrades to be purchased with white meeples, and simple VP collection with yellow meeples.  All of this is driven by a pleasant enough mancala mechanic.

Overall, I found the game okay though perhaps not particularly exciting.  Yes, if played seriously, it would be quite prone to AP.  And yes, I found the game seriously lacking in any theme; in that sense, it is very Feldian.  It is, though, a pleasant enough euro, willing, though certainly not eager, to play it again.

RATING: 6
While the meeple pickup and drop as breadcrumbs mechanic is interesting, it is targeted as an introductory game (note complexity and time ratings). The result, however, is that even knowing which options you have available is hard, since you must first calculate if they are even legal moves. Your head will feel exhausted like a marathon after calculating less than a handful of options, while deciding between options is the more trivial part. This is like having a hand full of cards... that need to be scratched thoroughly in order to reveal what they are.

If you are okay with having hard to find legal options then go ahead, but I'd rather have difficult choices between clear options than easy choices between obscure options. This game, more than any other, because of that mechanic, makes it very prone to inducing AP.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
Wow.  Simple rules, but surprisingly deep with lots of tense tactical decisions.

With other good players, it will be a constant battle to make sure that you can get the most points while minimizing their opportunities, simply by taking different paths across the board. (Don't leave single tribes by themselves, ESPECIALLY if it is a single work that can be targeted by an assassin).

There are many things that will give this game a significant amount of replay ability:

#1 Randomness of the tiles on the board
#2 Randomness of tribes placed on the tiles on the board
#3 Randomness of the market tiles taht come out
#4 Randomness of the genies that are available.  These have a significant influence since they have special powers, and can be exploited differently depending on when they are revealed or what the map layout looks like.

Overall, great game, and in my top 5 (possibly top 3) for GenCon 2014.

UPDATE: After a few more plays, this game has lost a bit of it's luster.  Though there is some strategy, it is mostly a tactical points optimization exercise and it is hard to plan ahead unless you are the first person to act since the board state completely changes after every player's turn.

Also, the AP between turns can be brutal.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 5
There is a lot of hype surrounding this game, and it just doesn't deserve it. It's not that it's a bad game, it's just not a great one. It's beautiful production values, designer pedigree, and multitude of mechanics dazzle gamers who can't see past them to the games flaws, the biggest of which is lack of choice. Certainly there are many things you can do on your turn, but only one optimal play. This is a game about staring at the board to find that optimal play. Unfortunately, with so many moving parts, finding that play can be difficult. Further, since the board is ever changing, you cannot spend your off-turn thinking about your play. Games can tend to devolve into a cooperative style play where each turn everyone works together to find the current players best move. Then at the end, points are counted and someone is declared winner. This will be a game that is mostly forgotten about in a year.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 9
Both in the production quality and game quality, this game is an absolute gem. It builds on the mancala mechanism by adding more modern gaming elements (set-collection, engine-building, area-control, etc) and still remains fairly easy to teach. The end result is a game with little luck, which provides a satisfyingly brain-burning experience. And where luck comes into play, the game has an ingenious auction mechanic that reduces it and maintains the fluidity of the game. Furthermore, it scales wonderfully 2-4 players. 

If there was any criticism to this game, is that it can slow down with AP-prone players... but that is only because how the rich the gameplay is. For euro/abstract fans, this is a must-play game.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
Days of Wonder is definitely back to gamers' games!

The usual stellar production value (wonderful unusual wooden pieces, fantastic artwork, sturdy cardboard), this time is paired to a meatier game with pretty simple rules but with great deep gameplay.

The set up is so variable, the not-fixed end conditions so varied, that interplay variability is guaranteed.

Players bid for turn order, then choose a tile, grab all meeples on it and drops them "mancala-style" on adjacent tiles. The last meeple must be of the same color of another meeple on destination tile: that color corresponds to an action. Then the player performs the action printed on the tile.

Is that simple, but the options are so many (auction-wise and mancala-wise) that analysis paralysis could be a serious problem: this game (more than others) needs to be played at brisk speed, since it's more tactical than strategic.

A great "Feldian" (and I mean it in a very positive way) point salad, that feels both innovative and classic, and plays smoothly like only a deeply polished and developed game can.

Two thumbs up!

RATING: 9

However, I couldn't give this the max rating due to its length of time to play a game, the strong tendency toward overanalysis of every move, and a somewhat steep learning curve (though the reference sheets included help a LOT).

RATING: 8
Five Tribes is a great game with one very specific problem.  At the start of a round you have to bid and in order to do that properly you have to look at the board and see how much you want to go first (or last), If you do not go first then the board state can often change enough that you will have to rethink your turn.  In addition there are a lot of options to think about on a turn.  For this game to work it really needs everyone to play fairly fast and loose but if this happens the game is great.  It does highlight what a genious Feld is in that his games have a similar level of richness but manage to avoid overwhelming players with choices by making only a smaller number available on each turn.  Also his games generally do not have such radical changes on the board between players.  Anyway the game itself all works brilliantly for a quick to teach but plenty to think about game.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
The core "pick-up and drop-off" mechanic is clever. It's immediately satisfying, it's addictive, it's gamer junk food. But like junk food, I'm not sure it's particularly substantial. The decision largely comes down to puzzling out the maximum point value move, rather than cleverly setting up a sequence of moves that work together in the aggregate. My feeling is that the two-player game will be far more interesting than any other iteration, due to the fact that you'll sometimes (often?) have two moves in a row (as will your opponent).

The Djinns and the resource cards both seem unnecessary, but I guess the five tribes had to have different ways of scoring.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 10
This game does everything RIGHT in my books. Components, replayability, long-term strategy, tactical gameplay, right amount of competitiveness, game time and minimal luck.

This game employs the Mancala mechanics and incorporates it into a 'reverse' worker placement game with slight auction element. The board is modular so there isn't a sure-win strategy every game, there's multiple pathway to victory. This game scales excellently from 2 players to 4 players, employing different strategies for different player counts.

The biggest complaint most players have is the issue of analysis paralysis since the board changes by the time it reaches your turn. I rarely have this issue as there's actually a lot of path you can consider in between turns. You have to be careful not to set up a good move for your opponent.

RATING: 9
Very solid Euro-style point-salad game where the main mechanic is moving meeples in a Mancala-style movement. Due to the game's nature, there's a high-degree of replayability. It's brain-burny without being overly long or arduous. Don't play this with AP people as it's too easy to stare at the board and get lost at the options. Bonus points for high quality art & components and a great box insert as well. The main negative to the game is it's very difficult to plan ahead for your turn since the board state changes with every move and you're forced to constantly re-analyze the situation. It's almost slightly chaotic in that way but I figure with more experience you get better at seeing the best moves.

Update (7/2017): 8.5 => 9
This game just keeps getting better for me each time I play it. The expansion makes it even better although it's not even necessary since the base game is that good. The beautiful production, the fairly short game play, the plethora of decisions, the multitude of mechanisms that synergize...it just works. The only negative I have still is the fact that it's difficult to plan ahead but it's not impossible. Always needing to reanalyze the board is part of the fun too.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6.5
A simple but interesting abstract game revolving around a Mancala-like mechanic.  I think it's too wide open at the start (what do I do?) and then too brain-burny towards the end.  The goal is to maximize victory points; often the best places to move to and from are fairly obvious, and the decisions are which tiles to move through and which color meeple to drop when moving through a tile.  You are continually thinking about how your move will set up or deny good moves for the next player(s); this makes the game interesting and tense but potentially painful if you play with AP-prone people.  It's not a game that I would seek out often, but it rates higher than several bland, abstract Euro games in my book.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 4
This is a provisional rating after 1 play, likely to drop if I play it again (I suspect, especially with more than 3 players!).

Mancala variant - which isn't a bad thing of itself I like Mancala and I like muliplayer mancala (ie. 3M's Oh-Wah-Ree).  This a pure abstract - theme is pasted on and makes little sense mechanic wise, and felt drier than sand (is that ironic being set in the desert?) with some complexity, both tactics and strategy (do you go for commodities or Djin or building or what) and point salad scoring added.

All probably a die-hard eurogamer's dream but not to my tastes at all.

Saying all that, if game play and fun factor were solid I'd look past it and want to play. 

What kills it for me is the downtime, don't ever ever ever play this with anyone with AP, unless you bring a book to read. You have to consider the board at the start of the round to bid for turn order, then consider the board when you play. *If* you go first, fine you've done the analysis, if on the other hand you're going later then the game state has changed often quite significantly by the previous player, so you have to start the thinking as you take your turn.

Eg, with 4 players and going 4th, forget considering your moves, just twiddle your thumbs while the 3 players before you sit in deep contemplation of their possible moves, it was bad enough playing 3 player with 2 fast opponents, I had an unpleasant mix of stress that I was taking too long and frustration at the downtime - and I won, which might mean I'm a genius at this game, or that there's significant luck, or it's deep, so really you need to consider your moves carefully ... thereby leading to more downtime.

And with 2 you get 2 actions a turn, so in theory you could manage 4 goes in a row (go last with both actions in one turn then first with both in the next) ... that seems rather powerful, and your opponent can go make lunch while your brain explodes with the possible combos you can do.

But I'm sure others who love solving a puzzle will get a kick out of it.

So, all in all - probably this is 3 or 2 for me as re-reading these comments I really don't want to play it again (but those ratings seem unfairly harsh, I probably need to give it another go, just not for a while)

RATING: 10
10x10 2015. This game takes about 3 or 4 plays to click but after it does, it becomes really good.  The first few plays I felt a little lost and there seem like there a ton of Djinns adding randomness.  But after a few plays you start to see the available moves and can evaluate them more quickly and the Djinns start coming up repeatedly and become well known.  The game is pretty tactical, you want to take the best move available while not giving easy moves to your opponent.  Chaining together moves and planning turns in advance is difficult, if not impossible, because your opponents will inevitably make unpredictable moves and take moves you didn't see.  Still, there is some long term strategy here in utilizing your Djninns, committing (or not) to collecting sets of resources, and trying (or not) to get the most viziers.  Initially, I thought variety seemed to be the way to go, and indeed if you have a really good move in front of you you should take it even if it doesn't fit with your plans.  But recently I've tried pursuing one or two goals with more focus from the beginning and found that to be a viable way to play too.  Anyway, the more I play the more awesome this gets- great game.

RATING: 7
Nice game
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
Wow!  One of my top 2 of Gencon.  Very simple rules but brain-burner player.  On your turn, pick up all the meeples in one space, then drop them mancala style on an orthogonal path.  The last space, the meeple dropped must match the color of a meeple in that space.  Pick up all of the meeples of that color and do that action of that color meeple.  Then, possibly optionally, do the action of that space.  Lastly, if there are no more meeples in that space (and no one else has claimed it previously) drop your marker on the space.  Points scores for a bunch of things at the game ends either when no more moves are possible or someone runs out of markers.  

Played with 3.  Probably took about an hour.  Easy to learn (and teach) but room for very clever play.  Very happy I got it.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: N/A
Played once, 2er.  This game is a puzzle in multiple ways.  It is literally a puzzle, and will (I think) appeal primarily to those who enjoy puzzles.  Finding the best move on the board is enjoyable.  Waiting for someone else to find the best move on their turn is not - which is the second puzzle.  This has clearly been designed for gamers, and yet it should absolutely not be played with gamers.  In order for it to clock in at a time that is not cumbersome, you will need to play quickly and intuitively.  I could not bring myself to do so.  There are multiple things to consider for every move, and many possible moves to consider on a turn, and I enjoyed puzzling out how to exploit groups of color on the board.  But I hated waiting for it to be my turn again.  And that was a 2 player game.  More players might ease some of the puzzling, since you aren't routinely planning 2 move combos, but it also increases the total number of turns you have to wait through. 

I should also mention that I definitely think that finding and evaluating possible moves is a skill that can be trained.  I expect that the playtime would decrease with experience.  But I am not willing to play several games with the hope that I will become proficient enough to play the game quickly.   

Overall, I came away happy that I had played this very unique design, but with no desire to own it.  I would play it again willingly if asked, but will likely not request it.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
Un estupendo juego que incorpora el mancala como su mecánica principal. En un tablero hecho del losetas empiezan en cada una de ellas tres meeples de hasta cinco colores que hay en el juego. En su turno, el jugador escoge una loseta y recoge los meeples y los va soltando a la vez que se desplaza sobre el tablero. Donde acaba realiza una acción gracias a los meeples (cada uno tiene un tipo de acción), y hace una acción de loseta. Además, también hay unas cartas para acumular mercancías y otras cartas de genios que te otorgan "poderes" especiales.
Es un juegazo. Sencillo de reglas pero con una profundidaz enorme. En cada momento hay multitud de posibilidades. Fue un gran descubrimiento.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 9.5
When the "slave controversy" first broke out, I was one of this game's biggest critics. I really wanted to try it because I knew this was my kind of game, but I also knew most of my friends would refuse to play. Since the slaves contribute absolutely nothing to the fun or thematic flavor of the game, I made [url=http://boardgamegeek.com/filepage/108201/replacements-slave-cards-camels]replacements for the slave cards[/url], and finally got my slave-free copy to the table.

And I'm so glad I did! This game presents some of the most dizzyingly complex and difficult decisions in a short time, with a very simple and elegant rule set that you can teach to just about anyone. Each game will be very different because everything in the setup (cards, tiles, meeples) is randomized, but otherwise the game is otherwise mostly open information. It manages to be both non-confrontational and incredibly competitive at the same time, providing an amazingly tense game experience. Possibly my favorite game of 2014.

RATING: 7
A really well designed game that's a bit overwhelming with choices at first, although it did get easier to find moves with subsequent play.

The basic mechanism is that you pick up all the pieces from a space, and then chain them through orthagonally adjacent spaces, dropping one meeple on each space you move through.  When you land on a space, you get some of the pieces there as well as get to take advantage of an ability printed on the tile.

The are a ton of ways to score points, which is cool, but the drawback to me is that there are just so many options for what you *could* do, that I found myself staring at the board trying to find the best option, which is odd for me, as I'm usually not an AP prone player.

I think my main issue that prevents me from rating it higher is that it's not a game you can play with your gut, as I usually play. You have to actually find the best possible move, which isn't something I find enjoyable.  I prefer games where I can calculate some basics, and shoot from the hip based on those calculations.  In this one, I have to look at a move, look at another move, look at another move, look again to see if there are any other moves that look better, etc, and you have a ton of potential moves.

Overall, still a really great game that I really enjoy but suspect a lot of people will like even better than I do.

RATING: 8
Five Tribes is the newest Days of Wonder prototype from designer Bruno Cathala. Unfortunately, the details of this game are still under wraps, so I am limited in what I can say.  What I can say: Five Tribes is a meaty, interactive, throwback Eurogame that I largely adored. It’s a bit of a pleasant surprise to see this one coming out of DOW, but I can’t wait to see what the finished product looks like on the table – I suspect it will be there frequently.

RATING: 8

What a fantastic game.  So much going on in this game, but it is all so intuitive, with multiple paths to victory.  It also has a Mancala mechanic that I have never seen used anywhere else, so to me it was incredibly innovative.  It matters what guys you move and how you move them and what spaces you claim and what you leave behind for other people.  The bidding for place is so important because someone can take your move... or even just screw up the placement on the board that it changes the move you were planning on making.  There is that worry of an AP player mapping out every move and the exact number of points it means to them, but I say you need to avoid those people in any game you play.  Definitely a keeper and a winner.

RATING: 9
Played twice with two players. Score for two players.  

The most brilliant thing in this game is that there is no luck involved at all. No dice and you don't draw random cards. It's purely strategic. I hate dice and avoid all games based on dice rolls so of course I love this. It's also different from all the games we previously own.

RATING: 5
This is a fragile game. I can't imagine playing with people who try to evaluate the best move on each play - it would take forever. Also, the game has an odd arc of restricting options as it goes along until the end when there may be 1 or 2 actions available total. It also has a bit of point salad feel, where there is a 7 wonders/Agricola like spreadsheet scoring at the end - the game does suffer from modern-euro disease of throwing too much at the wall. ie the djinns are silly and unnecessary and I would have probably removed them altogether. 

That said, I found the gameplay to this compelling. It plays fast with the right players (maybe an hour) and AP isn't a problem in the groups in which I play. It is very interactive, and it is a constant struggle to leave the worst board state possible for the following players. The turn-order auctions are critical. There is also competition for majorities, assassins etc. Solid effort. 

Update: I don't know about the turn-order auction. Getting at least 6-8 net points a turn seems standard, and it seems very difficult to make those points bidding 12 or 18. And bidding high and not getting to go first is an absolute disaster. Seems like bidding low, or 0 is much safer and a better play almost every time - certainly in the early rounds.

RATING: N/A

12/2016
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
12 times! That’s how many times I played Five Tribes during the first month, and that’s a huge amount of plays in my book (just take a look at my played stats for most of my games). Five Tribes is easy to teach and play, but at the same time it’s somewhat deep, although it’s highly tactical and not very strategic (you can perhaps have an overall strategy, but it’s impossible to plan your moves ahead). There are always a bunch of (mostly good) moves, so you really don’t want to play this game with friends who are prone to AP! The “mancala” mechanism can be quite brutal if you’re trying to figure out all possible moves… Also, there might be some AP in what to bid for turnorder, but I haven’t found it to be an issue.

There are various strategies to pursue, and none seem better than others, although it’s obvious that getting the majority of yellow meeples (viziers) is better in a 4p game than in a 2p game. It’s also easier to get a high numbered set in a 2p, although the card deck isn’t refreshed much if the same cards come out, so this deck can sort of stagnate (as can the djinn deck) unlike in the 4p game. Also, you better get those cards asap, because the green meeples disappear quickly… And, speaking of the meeples, I liked how they all have special abilities and how you need to elaborate which ones to activate. You need most yellow meeples for a bonus, you need some white in order to acquire some djinns (which are great – I love the variety of those), you need to be in your “spatial mind” when considering where the best places are to activate the blue meeples, you need to watch out for which cards the green meeples can give you, and finally you need to assassinate some meeples with the red meeples (either your opponents’ in order to, for instance, establish a majority in yellow, or to kill a single meeple on the board so you can place a camel). Speaking of camels, I think it’s great that the game can end in two ways, and due to the somewhat fixed game ending games never last too long.

For those of you who deny it, ties can happen! In 3 of my 2p games against my wife, we’ve ended within 4 points of each other although we pursued very different strategies, one of them being a 200-200 tie! Sure, you can say it’s somewhat of a “point salad game” and it doesn’t matter what you do as you get points anyway, but I don’t find it’s too much. All in all the balance between gameplay mechanisms, the amount of decision-making going on and the length of the game is very favorable! I’ve never had a game last for more than an hour, and that’s with 2, 3 and 4 players.

In two of my games I knew right away that I made a mistake concerning the amount I bid (I should definitely have made a higher bid), but that’s my own fault and I was eager to rectify my mistakes and want to play it again! Five Tribes is not as complex as some say, as in, yes, it’s more complex than some of DoW’s other titles like Ticket to Ride, but I find it way easier than, for instance, Mystery Express. It won’t be a hit with anybody as it’s quite themeless and dry and might cause AP, but it’s an overall enjoyable game which only lasts for an hour or less in my experience. The fact that my wife didn’t object when I suggested I would buy it speaks for itself!
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 8
Five Tribes is one of those games where the core mechanic really makes the game. The choice of picking up meeples, dropping them orthogonally to land on a spot, and then getting the meeple action and location action feels much more unique than even my uninspired description.

The closest game in spirit that comes to mind is Tzolk'in. Not because the mechanics are similar, but because the gears in Tzolk'in are unlike anything else. The rest of the game is pretty typical (collect resources, build buildings, etc.)

Five Tribes is kind of the same way. Very novel central mechanic, surrounded by some familiar things (set collection, some characters with powers and rulebreakers, etc.) I really enjoyed my first play, and I think this game probably has some longevity to it.

Update. One solo play as well, which is surprisingly good! I lasted the game but had a pretty weak score. 
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
Been looking at this since the initial video and really happy that it met my expectations and will be getting my own copy soon. The ideas in this game really shine through in a game that is easy to teach and play, but really makes you think. I thought there would be a lot of moving parts between the worker actions, tile actions, and djinn actions but they all fit together smoothly.

There is a chance for some AP in that the board can change a fair amount between turns so if you lose your move because of a displacement, then you may have to reevaluate the board on your turn. In addition, deciding what to bid for the auction can lead to AP although in our 3P game no one bid above 5 so it went pretty smoothly.

A lot to think about but super smooth rules, my kind of game! Can't wait to play again.

RATING: 7
A rather fascinating area control game using the Mancala mechanic for movement.  The game is also an application of controlled chaos -- since every move anyone makes can radically alter any of the plans you thought you had at the beginning of the turn -- mitigated by the risk/reward management of the bidding system.

The gameplay is varied and complex, the art is beautiful, and the components are of excellent quality.

RATING: 9.5
Great game from Days of Wonder that really puts them back on the map for me.

The basic mechanism is the Mancala mechanism.  You pick up all the colored meeples on a spaces and start dropping them off to adjacent spaces one at a time.  The space you end on, the color you end on, and the meeples currently occupying the space you end on all matter.
There are elements of set collection as well.
Overall, it combines to make a very unique Euro that is fairly challenging.

The theme is kind of thin, but the great components make up for it.  The biggest issue some people might have is the amount of AP this game can induce in players.  At the beginning of the game there are countless options for each player to take and ALL of them seemingly score points.

RATING: 10
So much fun. So much AP. 
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 4
2D Mancala with special abilities and a slew of ways to score points and thus play the game: Five Tribes in a nutshell.

Initially I put in a lot of effort to put a value to going first, second, whatever, but learnt the hard way that this is a complete waste of time and money/VP. There are far too many options to consider; and this doesn't even take into account the unpredictable nature of players going before you, changing the board to the point where your initial calculations are now useless. A replay confirmed this line of thought: bidding is only useful if you can be sure that you [i]profit[/i] from doing so... and that is only near the end, or when someone has been really careless (and others blind not to see it).

So, with a better understanding of the game under my belt I feel secure in talking about the game. The 2D Mancala core is kinda cool, if very abstract and not at all relating to any subject matter. Personally I don't give a damn whatsoever, but I can certainly imagine those of weaker spiritual disposition to be royally miffed at playing such a bare-bones abstract all of a sudden. I also like that the last colour you grab from the tile presents you the action you can take, if you like; and that emptying the tiles nets you points in the form of camels. So far so good. Unfortunately the author saw fit to overload this in principle very workable abstract concept with special action cards which break all the rules; set collection of resource cards to balance the weak tile values; a complete disregard for anything related to keeping a running score; and a complete lack of anything useful to do as other players are considering their actions.

It all adds up to Five Tribes promising to be something it cannot be. Admittedly the action and resource cards might, in the end, be not all that problematic as there is a decent selection of them, and it's not like they get taken in great amounts so that the core Mancala walking takes a noticable hit—at least not in my experience. But lack of a running score and interactivity now... those are [i]serious[/i] shortcomings, causing the game to become much simpler and less thoughtful than you'd think for such a ruleset. 

I will probably be made to play this title in the future some more, but I doubt it will improve much. It's a shame, really: there is definitely more in Five Tribes than is currently available.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9

Przepięknie wydana - śliczne drewniane znaczniki palm, pałaców, wielbłądów i członków klanu, piękne ilustracje dżinów.

RATING: 8.3
The latest release from Days of Wonder comes with good quality components, but it steps up the complexity from their previous games.

Five Tribes is a mix of mancala-type movement and trying to maximize the bonuses you can get with five different meeple types.  It's a tough challenge to plan out what you can get from the meeples and the tiles, but also hoping to clear out a tile and claim it for bigger points at the end of the game.  On top of that, there are some good abilities to be gained if you can sway a djinn to your side.

Lots of interesting choices, I definitely am a fan of this game already.  It also works to a relatively consistent endpoint, so it doesn't take too long to play.  I'd say the only downside is this will be tough for those prone to AP, particularly because the make-up of what's on the table is constantly changing.

RATING: 4
The 2-D Mancala is really interesting; the systems it's driving a bit less so. With strong AP this would be a nightmare, but I'm fortunate enough to have fellow players who are OK limiting their search depth (or using a turn clock).

But on a harsher note: while there are games out which do a decent job tackling the challenge of incorporating slavery into their mechanics, this isn't remotely one of them. The way it's handled tips me from "mild interest" to "don't especially want to play again".

(To be clear, it's not [i]the inclusion of slaves/slavery[/i]. Games can do that, and may have good reason to, especially if it's faithful to the setting/source material. It's the [i]way[/i] they're incorporated into this game that I find distasteful.)

[Edit: newer editions substitute Fakirs for slaves. You still buy them on the market alongside other commodities... hiring their services, one hopes?]

Every single game of Five Tribes is a brand new puzzle waiting to be solved, and that's what I particularly love about this euroabstract gem. The artwork and components are pure eye candy, the mancala mechanism is well implemented and the fact that everything after the setup is open information makes for a pretty cutthroat challenge. RATING: 7.9

Really recommended - lately we play with the Artisans expansion which offers even more possibilities to snatch a win.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
Five Tribes is essentially a Euro game that incorporates a two-dimensional mancala. There is a vague Arabian Nights theme permeating through the game and components but game play is pure Euro, meaning getting the most points. Every move on the mancala board leads to a few mandatory actions: one that depends on the color of the last meeple dropped, one that depends on the ending tile and potentially one more if the ending tile is empty. All of these give the player victory points or resource that will eventually be converted to victory points.

So how is it different from all the other Euro games? First, the artwork is superb and the colorful board is pleasing to the eyes. Second, each move changes the distribution of meeples on the board and thus the opportunity sets of the next player. The constantly shifting board makes it a challenge to plan ahead, so deep thinking and quick reactions are both needed to win. Lastly, the game is endlessly replayable due to the many pathways to victory points (owning tiles, collecting resource cards, using Djinn's special powers, etc.) and the random setup (tiles and meeples).

Five Tribes is not a casual or party game. You will think long and hard about each move but can never be sure it is the right one. You can try to specialize in one strategy but will be forced to adapt to the changing board. You can block your opponents but may open other, even better opportunities for them. The only thing you can be sure of is that you will not be bored playing it.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
Played 1x
Definitely not for everyone. This is a game that's hard to understand when you start playing. There are so many options! Choices go down over time, which is the opposite of most euros. 

One thing that makes it easier to see potential moves on the board is that if there is an EVEN number of meeples in a tile, you CANNOT end up in a tile that is an ODD number of spaces away, no matter what route you take, and vice versa.

I really like the puzzle aspect of this game and the elegant turn order bidding mechanism (which I'm going to use in Scoville from now on...) but had to trade away this game because it really wouldn't work with my group. Maybe I should buy it again now that there is an official solo variant...

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8.5
1-4 players, 45-75 minutes, medium weight, mancala/auction/area control, Bruno Cathala

https://boardgamegeek.com/thread/1520499/five-tribes-first-impressions

As most games of DoW this one looks great, has little rules and plays smoothly. The game could be considered a gateway game, but there is quite some strategy involved and at first glance the game may seem overwhelming.

The mancala-system is quite different from other games, which is refreshing and it works really well. Turn order is an auction/bidding, so you have to pay coins (=VP's) to be first in line, so you will not want to spend VP's unnessecary, but you would like to be first or second in line (depending on what's available on the board). If you choose to pay nothing, then you're placed in last position, but everyone after you can also choose to play nothing and move you even further back.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
Bidding victory points for turn order is a mechanism that normally demands more calculation than I enjoy performing. That problem is mitigated in this game, ironically, by devaluing the players' places in the order. Bidding low (and going late in the order) almost always works out, because the moves of the players ahead of you are likely to open up possibilities for you.
In any case, you get a point-salad Euro, the central Mancala mechanism of which is more interestingly employed in Trajan, in my view.

It's almost a luck-dependent game, in terms of "will I see a good move before the other players get annoyed with how long I'm taking?" You can easily imagine that a "best move" exists, but you can't guarantee that you'll find it. 

Definitely don't play this with anyone who's AP prone.

RATING: 6
This gets a 6 mostly on originality.  It has a little bit of the mancala mechanic where you pick up a bunch of workers, spread them across different tiles...taking the action on the last one based on the the color of the worker placed there.  Also the tile itself can carry a possible action.  Lots of different ways to score points.  

The big drawback with this game upon my first play is the game is extremely fragile to someone's poor play helping the next person.   I mention this because I didn't really think through one of my moves and how it would help the next player.  I left things wide open and basically handed the game to the next player.  This was a 4er game so I felt kind of bad for the other 2 players who lost.  I mean, I deserved to lose, but those guys didn't.  :P  So I think this game needs to be played with experienced players only otherwise someone random could be inadvertently crowned king.

RATING: 7.5
There's a lot to like about Five Tribes. You can start with the very unique mechanism by which you carry out your turn - picking up all the meeples and dropping them as you walk. You could go onto the beautiful art. You could wrap up with the excellent execution of the point-salad style (points are easy to track, despite being for a variety of different things.)

And yet, I find myself a little bored of it after having played it so much. I collect my 3 traders and get some valuable goods or use my assassin to clear my spot and another spot at the same time, and I think of how many times I've done exactly that before. It's still an excellent game, I just think I'm little worn on it.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6.25
Not a terrible game, but I don't get all the current buzz over it.  It's very thinky, you have to study the board carefully to identify possible moves, and you're never quite sure what plays everyone else sees/is going after.  I think the auction for turn order is pretty flawed because of this.  There isn't really a way to bid on the specific thing you want, other than trying to go first all the time, and due to the fixed bids and once-through order, there may not be a way to get yourself in the first 2 or 3 positions if the bids you wanted to take are gone.
I also think that collecting sets tends to be a pretty easy/reliable way to get tons of points, and getting camels out is also easy, and can shut the game down in a hurry if the other players aren't fighting for it.  The djinns which are the game's subtitle, otoh, are tough to get and kinda worthless.  If you grab them early,  (and they have a playable ability) you may be able to get some use out of them, but a lot of them are kind of useless.  The blue guys will have perhaps 1 or 2 good plays during the game if things line up right, but are largely an afterthought.  Lastly, the palm trees and palaces rarely amount to anything.
So, although the game can have decent replayability due to the modular/random setup and due to the different playstyles of your groups, the actual moves you're making fall into narrow, repetitious patterns.

RATING: 7

[thing=157354][/thing] is a very enjoyable game.  I can't say that it is as great as some of the buzz around it but it is a pleasant game and works well.   Like Puerto Rico you need to be careful of player positioning in this game.   A player who just hoovers up stuff without thinking about the position he is leaving the board in for the next player will give game-winning benefits to the player on his left.  In fact the game really becomes as much about containing the opportunities for the next player as maximising your own.  And so turns can take a while as you evaluate the benefits to you of each of the large variety of potential options available and you evaluate the benefit to the next player of the same.   It is this that prevents it from moving from a Good Game into a Great Game from my perspective.   I don't suffer from AP and tend to play fast and intuitively (which may be why I lose a lot!) but playing Five Tribes in that manner can create problems. 

Don't get me wrong, played without thinking too much and Five Tribes will move quickly and enjoyably, but ultimately the winner will be determined more by the board state the winner received on each of their turns rather than the game they played on their turn.  

It's a keeper for sure and one I'm looking forward to testing further over the coming months.

RATING: 9
 fővel próbáltuk nagyjából 1,5 óra volt, 40-80 percet írnak, stimt.
A játékelemek gyönyörűek, DoW minőség. Nyelvfüggetlen.
A játék: végig izgalmas.
A játék menet:
Licit a körsorrendért – van egy előre meghatározott értékkel (0, 1,3,5,8,12,18)feltöltött licitsáv, ide lehet a kör sorrend szerint letenni a bábukat, ezzel licitálva. 0-sából 3 is van, aki legkésőbb tesz a 0-sra az jön előbb a 0-sok közül, egyébként értelemszerűen a legmagasabb értéket megtevő kezdi a körét. Plusz amilyen sorrendben játsszák le a körüket a játékosok, olyan sorrendben fognak majd a köv. fordulóban licitálni.
A játékos akciók: a játékos egyben végig csinálja a körét. Ebben a sorrendben:
kiválaszt egy lapkát, amin vannak figurák, felveszi őket, és egyesével lepakolja őket, miközben lépked lapkáról lapkára. (klasszik mancala). A trükk: olyan helyre lehet befejezni, az utolsó figurát letenni, ahol van olyan színű figura, mint amit odatennél.
Ahol befejezte a játékos a lépését: onnan felveszi az összes olyan színű figurát, ami megegyezik azzal, amit odatett. Ha kiürül a lapka ettől: akkor megszerzi -1x lehet megszerezni, és nem lehet átvenni, a végén pontot ad.
Végrehajtja a felvett bábuknak megfelelő akciót.
5 törzs, 5 féle figura,
sárga: csak gyűjt, a végén pont;
fehér gyűjt, a végén pont, ha nem használja el a dzsinnek megidézésre;
zöld: árut szerez, annyit amennyi bábut felvett,
kék: érméket szerez (annyit ahány kék lapka a környéken, szorozva a felvett kék bábuk számával, ez a szorzó növelhető rabszolgakártyával (kék bábu+rabszolga= szorzó)));
piros: gyilkol: vagy a tábláról –ezzel akár újabb területet lehet szerezni –lapka-, akár ellenfél sárga vagy fehér bábuját.
Ezután jön a lapka akció végrehajtása: ahol befejeztük a pakolást, azért kell/lehet végrehajtani. 5 féle lapka, 2 kötelező végrehajtású, 3 opciós.
A 2 kötelező a lapka értékét növeli,
a 3 opciós egyike a dzsinn szerzés, ami jó sok pontot ad, illetve spec képességeket a játék közben. A másik 2 opciós végrehajtásúval árukat lehet szerezni.
Ami fontos: a forduló közben nincs feltöltés, ami elfogy, az elfogy.
Jó sok mindenért lehet pontot: lapkák, dzsinnek, sárga bábuk –itt is egy trükk: nem csak a bábukért, hanem 10 pont/ olyan játékos, akinél több sárga bábud van -, lerakott plusz elemek a te lapkádra (pálma, palota), árukártya sorok –különböző elemekből kell állnia, jó sok pont lehet belőle, ezeket játék közben is lehet váltani, ha pénzre van szükség.
A játékunk:
A licit: első fordulóban: max 3-as érték, aztán ahogy éreztünk rá, illetve inkább, h akartunk elvenni biztos sok pontos lépéseket, úgy ugrottak be a 12-es 18-as értékű licitek.
Előretervezés: 3-4 fordulóra nem lehet. 2-re igen. És érdemes is. mert h előre tervezni, h mennyire licitálj, h vajon más is hasonlót kurkászott ki magának, és akkor mennyit is licitálj, h ne menjen föléd. Ugyanakkor nagyon érdemes figyelni arra ki mit lép, mert egy-egy lépéstől sok-sok új lehetőség nyílik meg és ilyenkor érdemes lehet tervet változtatni.
Jelzem tök utolsó lettem, de végig élveztem. Ugyanis végig izgalmas, állandó tervezést igényel, és újratervezést. A szabályok egyszerűek, de a mancala zseniálisan van beépítve, a pálya folyamatosan változó, a mancala miatt, és rengeteg helyről lehet pontot, és kérdés, h épp mire merre, menjünk rá. Szóval lenyűgöző. –legalábbis engem :-)-
Erősség: vhol a Kennerspiel felső vége.
Egy baja az 1-4 :-)
A vége nálunk: 1-2. hely: 126-117 pont, a harmadik-negyedik: 88-84.
Szóval szoros csaták, menetközben kevésbé látható, legalábbis egy játék alapján, bár érezhető ki, h áll. (Nincs közbeni pontozás, a végén egyben minden, de a pénzek és az áruk kivételével minden látható, az árukat meg nagyjából lehet figyelni)
Nekem nagyon bejött. 
Update: még mindig tetszik, igaz most sem tudtam a top2-be kerülni :-)

RATING: 4
If I ever play this again, it's because I'm being asked to fill a spot at the table.  This is simply not my cuppa.  That's not to say it's a bad game, because clearly a lot of people enjoy it, but so many things rubbed me wrong with Five Tribes and I can't even accurately put a finger on what it was.

It could have been the down-time (which I don't mind in wargames), the AP of other players (which I don't mind in epic games), the bidding mechanic (which I don't mind in financial/market games), the random chaotic nature of camels, temples, oases, or whatever (which I don't mind in, well, everything - chaos rocks), maybe the somewhat unbalanced djinn powers?  I dunno.

To me, the game simply wasn't fun.  Even with terrible games, I can find something fun in it, but this felt very much like solving a puzzle where other people are hiding your pieces.  Even though it has all these elements that I normally don't have an issue with, for some reason Five Tribes combines them in such a way that I simply don't want to play it again.

RATING: N/A
Erlangen  schwer lang
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
Five Tribes is a current favorite of mine. It's primarily a tactical game (over a strategic one), but I don't think that's a bad thing. It's often better to ditch your strategy to take advantage of points immediately on the board, so continual evaluation/re-evaluation of your plan is necessary and keeps you on your toes. Set collection can be one of the higher point scorers in the game, but it can be risky, too, for the same reason. Moving the meeples around is a fun puzzle (how do you land with the color you want, on the square you want, while also thwarting the next person's turn?). 

My one "criticism" of the game is that there's a fair amount of "luck." Five Tribes is great if all the players are on the same level - both thinking about their own moves the moves others' want to make, and finding the optimal balance. However, one new player (thinking only about his/her own moves) can destroy the balance of the game, as they unwittingly (a) leave the best moves on the board, or (b) set up an excellent move for the next player. Just something to be aware of; if playing with new players, be prepared to sit back and relax, and take the game as un-seriously as possible.

RATING: 6
[b]Rating based on - 15+ plays, 1 player and 2 player[/b]
By all accounts I should :heart::heart::heart::heart::heart: this game. So why don't I?

:heart: The Euro-ness
:heart: The Abstract-ness
:heart: The GORGEOUS art and graphics
:heart: The wonderful production quality
:heart: The unique meeple movement mechanism and the puzzle it provides.

However, [b]every [/b]time I look at the box I [b]dread [/b]playing it. For all of the great things there is one thing that takes the joy out of the entire game for me - bidding for turn order :yuk: It's not just me. The Mr. hates it too. He refuses to bid anything other than 0...unless I bid 0 then all of a sudden he's willing to spend money. There were a couple games he won just bidding 0 and that annoyed me no end and soured the game for me. When he used that tactic in auction Tikal he lost, and I felt this game rewarded him for doing nothing. It's not the game's fault. We do not like any kind of bidding/auction in games.

In any case, the designer came out with an official solo variant that really made this palatable for me. In my first solo game, like the score chart said, it was almost too easy. I definitely played it correctly. The rules are simple, and Yazid is easy to implement. Even with quite a few sixes coming out I got a major score on my first game which I wasn't expecting. Didn't get a chance to play it more solo and wouldn't keep it for only solo play - too much setup.

RATING: 9.5
It's always difficult to play a game like this the first time. You have thousands of opportunities at the start of the game with a descending number of opportunities as the game goes by. Playing with players who want to analyze everything may easily result in too much downtime.
On the good side, you get a real kick out of this when finding the perfect way to move your meeples and score a lot of points.
This game really grows on you after repeated plays. Exploring the different strategies, getting constant kicks from boosting points, finding a smart path that at the same time destroys your opponents' opportunities, etc. A lot of good stuff going on, and the playing time is really acceptable.
Definitely something original, and some awesome components! Great game, but never destroy a game like this by picking opponents with "analysis-paralyses" tendencies.

RATING: N/A

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7.85
When I first purchased Five Tribes, my main concern was that the game would get boring, as we intently stared at the board in a wash of AP.  In our early games, we didn't know enough about the strategy to do so, so the game moved quickly and felt exciting.

The more we've played it, however, the stronger the AP has become.  We've seen multiple paths to victory all work well, so now we are looking for more types of board conditions.  It really slowed the game down.

This is one where I think we'll take a break from playing it for a while, intentionally giving ourselves time to forget some of the strategies so we can play "from the hip" rather than carefully analyzing everything.

I will say that, despite the randomized boards, randomized cards, and randomized genies, by the tenth play the game was starting to feel a bit samey.  Even though there were many different paths to victory, none of them really seemed to stand out as having a different [i]feel[/i] to them.

However, with all of that negative stuff being said, this is a game that is shockingly well balanced, considering how much is going on.  Many different paths to victory can work in a given game, and strong tactical play can snatch a come-from-behind win if you make your move at just the right moment.  The puzzle of this game feels very unique, and the pasted-on theme somehow does manage to give it just enough flavor to not feel completely abstract.

Also, I still have to say it - the choice to include the slave cards bothers me, and many plays of the game haven't changed that belief.  Especially when teaching the game at meetups to people I don't know, I always cringe when I get to the part that I have to try to make excuses for the crass inclusion in an otherwise very abstract game without a lot of adherence to the theme.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 5
One 2-player game
A mishmash of elements that mostly works. I was prepared to hate the puzzle element of it but it's not bad. In my 2-player game there were a fair number of move choices but usually your target(s) seem pretty obvious. It's mostly a matter of trying to not set up the board for your opponents to score easily or have easy choices.

Seems like there are too many resource grabbing tiles and they were under-utilized in my game. There are far juicier targets. Are they meant to be generic consolation prizes? Probably more occupied/used with more players.

Lastly, I have to agree with a GeekBuddy's opinion that the auction for turn order mechanism is largely useless since only the winner really knows what they're getting. The board can be very different for subsequent players (less so for two players).

I think this is probably a very different game with two players versus more players.

RATING: 5
There are over one hundred different meeple moves that you have to choose from.
When it is your turn, you can
1) Calculate which of those potential moves is legal and will give you most winning points. If everybody does that, one game will take at least three hours.
2) Quickly choose a meeple move that seems good to you. But this way, you will be annoyed because later you realize that there would have been a better move and you overlooked it.

RATING: 6
Mancala meets euro-salad, and has an AP-prone boardchild.

Rating history: 7|6|4

2p - I like this best as a 2p duel. (1x)
4p (1x) Not much control, too much downtime.

[b]4th play, Jan 2016.[/b] My interest and enjoyment of this game ticked up just a bit. When I disconnect from the need to find the best play, and just settle for a good move, and more good moves, playing casually, I enjoy it.

1st play: 7. Oct/14. More euro-style than I thought it would be, but I also liked it more than I thought I would. 2p game playing assassins a bit incorrectly, but fun to build camel chains to make opponents moves more difficult. Enjoyed.

2nd play: 6. Same night. The 4p game had a lot of downtime between turns (3 waiting turns instead of 1), and harder to play defense, plus a weak player can leave lots of good opportunities on the board for stronger players. Also find the djinns to lack variety (i.e. not enough of them).

3rd play: 4. Months later (July 2015), I teach this to some friends to see if my "meh" reaction was just game snobbery. Brandon's reaction: "It's something different, but it not a game I'd go out of my way to play." Couldn't have said it better myself.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8.5
Typical Days of Wonder quality and polish to this game - it looks amazing.  

The gameplay is very simple "Mancala-like" by movement of groups of coloured meeples which must be dropped off one by one on adjacent tiles as a pathway is made.  The colour of the final meeple must match at least one meeple from the final tile and it's that colour which determines your meeple action.  The colour represent different clans such as white=Elder, red=Assassin etc.  Each has a different action.

It's quite addictive as you search out the best movement options and hope the other players a) don't spot it and b) don't ruin it by parallel movements.

It is prone to AP players if they insist on calculation all the possible options for the meeple movement so choose your group carefully!
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 6
Five Tribes is a game with pretty simple rules but not quite as simple to play in practice. That's not because the game would present you with particularly interesting dilemmas that need to be pondered but solely due to the size of the decision tree you're asked to grapple with each turn.

There is hardly any strategic play possible in the game. The board changes significantly between turns and the only task is to maximize returns for the current turn which mostly involves a lot of scanning and rescanning the board for opportunities. Beware of AP here.

To compare it to another DoW game, it is quite a bit less strategic than Ticket to Ride, yet more demanding in turn-to-turn play. I'd have preferred a less tactical approach since the underlying mechanism does show some promise.

RATING: 10
Oh this game, this game... I do not know where to start. Five Tribe is unique, strategic euro game. Thanks to modular game board and random meeple setup is every game completly different. It is full of options and you have to find a new possibilities after yours opponent turn. In the most cases long term strategies does not work and you are forced to play a react kind of a game. Five Tribes is for an specific audience, not for everybody. If you like heavy strategic games, euro games, buy your copy today!

RATING: 6
Massive overproduction aside, the Mancala mechanism is novel enough, but it ultimately creates an intensely tactical scenario due to the way the board state changes so drastically every turn. The optimization of every turn seems nearly infinite which means moves must be frustratingly taken regardless of their absolute quality so that the game might end before the players' lives do.
The auction for turns every round is necessary, but makes the game feel even slower than it already is.

RATING: 8
Quite a good game. Very Euro, but brings something new to the table with its mechanic of "worker drop-off and removal". This is a great-grandchild to mancala. There are many ways to score (point salad) but players may wish to only focus on a few and hope to maximize. The components are GLORIOUS and almost worth the price of admission. This is a great game for people who like medium-weight Euros. This game could seriously fall into an AP spiral for new players.  There are always about 10-15 moves one can make, and there is always 1 optimal, best move. For players who really want to do the math, everyone may find themselves sitting around waiting for the turns to complete. So, not revolutionary, but a well-executed design that will not appeal to everyone - but it certainly appeals to me!

RATING: 7
[b]Solo Rating: 5.5[/b]

Using official solo rules published by the game designer subsequent to release:

https://cdn0.daysofwonder.com/five-tribes/en/img/ft_solo_en.pdf

[b]Recommended player count: 3-4[/b]

Although popular with many, I'm not keen on the 2 player rules which over complicate the game in my opinion.

[b]Comments:[/b]

You might want to steer clear if prone to analysis paralysis as the options on your turn seem endless and you can't really plan ahead as the board state can change quite dramatically (and in unpredictable ways) on other player's turns.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 5.5
Fairly abstract, puzzle type game.  If you enjoy theme, pass.  Action selection is similar to mancala, but you have several goals: get the meeple action you want (last color you drop), get the tile action and possible tile points (where you end up), and don't create good moves for your opponent(s).  In addition, with a once around bidding system for turn order, you would ideally consider the differential in payment during the bid.  Game is enjoyable and challenging.  If there was a theme with well integrated mechanics, I'd probably enjoy it a lot more.  It does have a potential A/P problem, and downtime in waiting to see what the board looks like for your turn.  Very nicely produced.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 2.5
I intensely disliked this game. It suffers from a design that encourages timeless inaction as other the peoples' moves make planning your own almost impossible.  Matters are made worse with pieces of similar size and colors, all becoming a jumbled mess.  At some point, trying to decipher the meeples from the player tokens and the palm trees and all the other items that clutter the board become a huge headache.  Literally.  Even ending this painful experience was difficult;  there is too much info to track and scoring is complex and non-intuitive.  A scoring app is almost a necessity.   Scoring is variable with in-game Genie cards modifying many of the totals.  Paper/pen/calculator are needed.

Really, this is one of the most unpleasant games I have experienced in quite some time.  Mid-game I was standing and pacing, wanting desperately to end the torture.  You could not pay me to play this again.

RATING: 6
Days of Wonder's first "Gamer's" game and first attempt at Euro-style mechanics.
THE SHORT:
It works flawlessly, but, like real Euro-games, it doesn't really capture a theme like other legendary Days of Wonder titles. If you want a Euro experience, there are several better titles. If you're trying to coax your game group toward something other than the latest zombie themed Ameri-trash, then this might whet their appetite, but don't expect them to stampede toward AGRICILA or CAVERNA after this experience. FIVE TRIBES, like its desert setting, is a little on the dry side. You will respect the mechanics, but its fun factor is low.
THE LONG:
Days of Wonder games are great at theme: [thing=10630]MEMOIOR '44[/thing] perfectly captures the spirit of WWII battels, [thing=15062]SHADOWS OVER CAMELOT[/thing] drips theme, [thing=9209]TICKET TO RIDE [/thing] is all about building that long rail line, and [thing=40692]SMALL WORLD [/thing] gives you the impression of civilizations rising and falling at your God-like whim.
However, during our playthrough of [thing=157354]FIVE TRIBES[/thing], I never once felt like a sultan. I didn't feel like I was interacting with tribal politics, and even with powerful Djinn at my beck and call, I never felt their mystic wonder. The only thing that made me feel somewhat connected to the Egyptian or Persian aspect of this game was the Mancalla-like mechanic of picking up a group of meeples, and then distributing them in a linear fashion around the tiles. 
WHICH IS COOL!  It creates a constantly evolving puzzle that each player is trying to solve at the same time. This puzzle has so many moving parts and so many ways to earn points, that you may not want to play this with certain strategy buffs who will sit for 20 minutes trying to choose the best move for point yield.
There is a lot of praise out there for Five Tribes, and when a game is this good, the theme doesn't matter as much. Five Tribes is similar to Dominion, in that it's a solid design with game play that constantly changes and challenges each person to play it in different ways, but the theme is as thin as ink on paper. 
So, yes, I understand that you don't always need theme to have a good game, but, Five Tribes just doesn't feel "engaging" to me.  There is very little excitement, and after agonizing over every move and what strategy to follow, you don't exactly know how your strategy payed off in the end, which makes for one of those "Oh, it's over?" anticlimactic situations. Most times, you won't know who won until MATH happens, as each player spends a few minutes counting all their points, similar to [thing=68448]7 WONDERS[/thing]
I do have nice thing to say about Five Tribes: it scales very well from 2 to 4 players, and did I mention the lovely components? But none of that puts it over the top for me. My desire to play again is more of an effort to understand it's many-faceted, shifting, victory point system, which is brilliant. Perhaps that IS the theme, a puzzle that moves and changes like majestic sand dunes. If that's the case, then I'd have to say "well done" to designer [person=1727]Bruno Cathala[/person] and his play testers. Sorry I missed it.

Ultimately, FIVE TRIBES is one of those VERY NICE games in my library that sit there asking me why I didn't commit to something more like [thing=120677]TERRA MYSTICA[/thing] or [thing=105551]ARCHIPELIGO[/thing]. It's a purchase that once again convinces me that as far as nerd culture is concerned, I'm definitely a member, but I'm just not trying hard enough.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 8
Very interesting use of the "Mancala" mechanic. There is a multitude of possible moves every turn until near the end, each one changing the game state, creating and removing other possibilities. Add an auction for turn order and it's easy to see how the game can be quite a brain burner if taken seriously, causing "analysis paralysis" even in some players that are not prone to that. However, with multiple ways to score, most very different from each other, it's very rewarding to explore its many strategies.
