=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 5
Un juego con el tema pegado, es un euro abstracto con mecánica de mancala y ensalada de puntos que es el sueño húmedo de los Eurogamers duros.

Los componentes a la altura: kilos de madera y mepples encima de fichas de cartón de colores pasteles.

Hasta ahora es el juego de Cathala en caja grande que mas me ha gustado, lo cual no es mucho decir. 

El principal problema del juego son los tiempos muertos, es un juego muy propenso al AP, hay que estudiar el tablero para buscar combos y ver si quieres ganar la subasta y si eres el primero ahí terminas, pero si eres el segundo o el tercero, o a lo peor, eres el cuarto, resulta que el tablero ha cambiado tanto o te han levantado el combo que habías visto inicialmente que toca volver a analizar y repensar la jugada que quieres hacer.

Por otro lado aunque cada partida el set up es totalmente diferente, tengo la sensación de que con varias partidas es mas fácil reconocer y jugar patrones.

Es un Euro de alma familiar digno de la escuela mas dura, y hará las delicias de auqeloos a los que les encante resolver este puzzle, yo no me encuentro entre ellos. Prefiero euros mas elegantes, con menos tiempos muertos y a poder ser mucho mas dinámicos en su desarrollo.

Con 2 jugadores moviendo cuatro peones tiene que ser el infierno AP buscando combos ganadores, y con 4, mejor llévate un libro para leer entre los turnos, por lo menos hasta que la gente sepa jugar y vea las jugadas y haga los movimientos en automático.

Y esto, es el mejor de Cathala en caja grande.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 7
This game starts with so many options!  It's definitely a candidate for analysis paralysis, or for computerization to allow all options to be visible.  It's often not clear what is the best option at a given time.  Sometimes there is one clearly better square... but only if you see a way to get there from another square (sometimes by going in a loop and hitting the square twice).

Playing with 3 players is difficult enough, but 4 players would probably be bad, because you'd never be able to plan your turn in advance if you didn't go first.

The bidding leads to painful decisions.  You can be stuck in a position where if you bid high, you can win but everybody else will save lots of money; if you bid low, you won't win; and if you bid zero, you'll be leaving a good option to someone else for a ridiculously low price.  It's an interesting (and sometimes painful) mechanic that bidding zero determines that you will go later than any player who's still in the bidding.

The scoring ends up being over 150 points, which I think is too high.  It's hard to value points when there are so many available!  There are obviously many ways to get points and win, so it's a matter of balance.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 4
Five Tribes is unmistakeably a game that has evoked and will evoke a diverse range of opinions (and I'm not even talking about the inclusion of slaves in the game). Mechanically, the game puts to work a mancala style movement on an board of 30 spaces (5x6 tiles) rather than a closed linear loop - this means that when you pick up meeples (not stones! - there are also multiple colours), there are multiple possible locations to which your movement can end (including doing loops) as well as decisions to make about which meeples you drop off on route. This, in turn, creates an immense tactical horizon which becomes paralyzing to some. Now this is not what I take issue with here, I'm usually a fan of brain-burny games (and huge one at that). However, the intellectual effort required to grind through the possibilities does and the tactical outcomes do not seem to be in sync -- almost everything generates points, and very few outcomes seem to deny or heavily deviate other players plans in a consistent or meaningful way. In other words, I find myself doing the heavy lifting of a game like Reef Encounter (i.e., heavy and meaningful game-shaping actions), but with the outcome of Castles of Burgundy (i.e., if I do this, I get two additional points and my opponent will have to implement another approach which will likely result in a few points less). While this sounds hyper-critical, I still think that the game itself offers something new and creative -- I simply wish that it offered a bit more granularity or fewer paths to victory to really ramp up the competition (especially in light of how taxing the game is).

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7.75
Five Tribes is a clever activation game requiring small caravans of meeples to move about the board in order to activate and claim tiles.

Players bid for turn order each turn, and then move meeples on the board in a path from the location where they started as mancala stones.

At the destination matching meeples activate, and the tile activates- possibly a player can claim the tile as well, and then may take optional actions.

Because the mancala motion occurs on a shared grid instead of a ring or a personal board, meeples left behind can help or hinder other players from their next action- it is a very interactive game and moves quickly (as long as players do not try to analyze/optimize too long).

In another clever twist, all bidding and goods purchasing is done in coins which are also VP, so players do need to judge whether the expense will provide bigger returns in the short run, or be a poor investment.

It's very understandable why this was an award-winning game- the elements work together well in an integrated whole, and come to a timely but challenging conclusion.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
(02/20/2015) Comment based on one play.

A mancala-like sowing game, where sowing is not fixed in one circular direction, but occurs on a grid. Sowing can occur horizontally or vertically, where some action(s) can occur on the last sown space, based on the type of meeple sown, as well as the action printed on the space.

The board consists of tiles comprising a 5x6 grid, where 90 meeples of five different colors (18 meeples in each color) are randomly distributed, with each space containing 3 meeples. The board itself is randomly generated, where each grid space is assigned a random tile, each with differing VP values and optional actions.

On their turn, a player picks up all the meeples in any one space and then "sows" one meeple in every space they traverse away from the original space. The player collects all the meeples of the color of the last sown meeple, triggers whatever action (if dictated) is dictated by the color, and then lastly can execute the optional action of the space.

If they happen to end up in a space that has removed the last meeple(s), they can then put a camel in that space, claiming it for its VP value, which will be summed for endgame scoring.

The five tribes/meeple types represent different things in gameplay: 1) Yellow represents Viziers. Collecting the most Yellow represents VP at the end of the game; 2) White represent Elders, which can be turned in to summon "Djinns" (genies) that give extra capabilities; 3) Green represents Merchants, which allow acquiring of commodity cards that score much like the civilization cards in Stone Age; 4) Blue represents Builders that score depending on board position; and 5) Red represents Assassins that allow removing meeples at a distance, which is useful for claiming empty tiles.

When the rules were explained to me, my reaction was: "Is that it?" Given the random starting conditions, it is a matter of the players to identify and execute the best sowing pattern(s), and improve either short-term or long-term scoring possibilities. Based on this, players conduct a once-around bid to determine player order. Spend most, go first. Spend least, make first bid in the next round. As the number of meeples is reduced through sowing, the number of best sowing pattern(s) will change and shrink. Near the end of the game, there are very few sowing patterns left. The key is identifying the best sowing pattern(s) and bidding high enough (in VP) in order to get first-crack at exploiting the patterns.

So, since the board is all open-information, it is possible to "find" (or solve) the best sowing pattern. Then, it is a matter of bidding the proper amount to exploit it, and hoping the other players don't recognize the same pattern that you have. Due to the monstrosity of choices available, this is probably the source of AP that others have commented about. There may be some tactical considerations when sowing, to ensure that players after you are not given some better sowing pattern. There seems to be little strategic implications, and certainly no satisfying combinations to build up to, since the board changes after every player's turn. Even with all the thinking and rethinking to maximize points, the end result of taking no more than one or two trivial actions is a letdown. Given all this, I find this "searching for best patterns" abysmal as a game mechanism. It's kind of like "memory" as a game mechanism in Concentration--it may have been fun when I was very young, but now find it boring. The same goes for the game--it didn't get me excited, and I found myself quickly bored.

The production values are gorgeous, as it is standard Days of Wonder fare. The game is accessible and not very difficult to play, although there is plenty of depth here if you want to delve into the many interacting pieces. It's just the main mechanism leaves me flat, and the payoff is simply not worth the effort. Initial rating is a "6". I am willing to give it another try, but I am not terribly excited about it.

RATING: 7
[b]tl;dr[/b] — Light & fast = FUN; serious & slow = OH GOD KILL ME NOW

[b]After 1 play:[/b]  [COLOR=#979797](2p)[/COLOR]  Works well as a chill activity with a glass of wine & some downtempo ambient.  It's pretty, has neat little puzzles to solve, and everything you do nets you some kind of return, so there's a feeling of constant forward progress (as opposed to irritating impotence).  I like the dark undertones of Rasputin-like manipulation of entire peoples, murder, and slave sacrifice in order to treat with demons.  But that's just me.

I think it would be [i]absolute hell[/i] with hardcore gamers, especially at max player count.  Each turn can be laboriously optimized for maximum payoff with minimum setup for the next player; but this process, at least in the early game, involves brute-forcing all possible moves to climb the various branches of the game tree, a fractally-fuzzed boredom bush.  This might be okay if you're playing against quantum computers (spoiler alert:  you will lose), but it would be excruciating with savannah-born meat-brains (chuck a spear, check; grind [i]n[/i]-ply game states in a timely fashion, not so much).  A chess clock would be a must... or you could just hold everyone to casual play and save the thinky for heavier games where such deep contemplation is part of the intended experience.

Probably most enjoyable with your honey, family play, or two couples as a social activity.

[b]Thoughts on the 2p rules:[/b]

It's a bummer that the process of turn-order bidding for two is merely implied in the rules—maybe more obviously for some, but still.  It would have been nice to have a small, concrete example of what must otherwise be intuited.

[q="No one"]
The two players will bid for [i]four[/i] turn order slots; it is therefore possible, through clever and/or aggressive bidding, to get two (or more!) turns in a row.*


[size=7]*If you go 3,4 in one turn and then 1,2 in the next that's [i]four turns in row![/i][/size][/q]
[b]After 4 plays:[/b]  [COLOR=#979797](3x 4p)[/COLOR]  It's a solid 7.  Works as intended—a pleasant little puzzler with lots to do, Nerf®-edged back-and-forth, and gorgeous to boot.

[b]Stuff we got wrong:[/b]

• [i]Resource cards are hidden.[/i]
Good luck finding that in the rulebook...

• [i]Djinn & the market only refresh between rounds.[/i]
What you see during turn order bidding is all you'll get until next time...

• [i]Viziers score 1 VP each, then +10[/i] per person[i] with fewer than you.[/i]
So if the final vizier count is 5, 4, 3, 1 the scores will be 35, 24, 13, and 1.  This will crazy-change the game...

[imageid=2537768 original]
[size=7][i]"Shaitan take these infidel rules!"[/i][/size]

RATING: 8
A fun, colorful, dynamic point-salad game. There is a bidding process (I hate bidding) to get to go first; mancala-type board "movement"; a variety of board actions based on where you end your turn on the board and therefore lots of strategy involved in where you want to go; Djin cards to give you abilities, a market to collect sets to gain points... it's a lot of stuff that somehow comes together to create a coherent game. It kid of ends up having a "something for everyone" feel to it in this way. And best of all, the 2-player game works VERY well with the double-turns, unlike some board games that weaken with fewer players.

RATING: N/A
規則
1.競標
2.行動階段: 
 A.收米寶
 B.移動米寶 (同一格不能放兩次，對角算兩步，
                                 最後一個須有相同顏色米寶)
C.區域控制判定(清空放駱駝)
D.使用米寶能力  
E.使用板塊能力(必須)

米寶能力: a.藍色(建築師):拿錢幣:計算周圍藍色板塊數量X拿起米寶數量 
                  b.紅色(暗殺):殺死版圖上一隻米寶(距離看紅色數量判定，一    
                     隻一格)，或者殺死敵人米寶，板塊被清空一樣放駱駝。
                  c.綠色(商人): 拿取幾個就拿幾張貨物卡(從前面算)
                  d.黃色: 最後一之1分，數量贏一位玩家就+10分
                  e.白色(長者):最後一支兩分，用來招喚神燈。
最後計分:1元一分，板塊控制分數(棕欖樹+3，皇宮+5)，卡片種類(算套) ，白色黃色米寶

RATING: 10
Awesome Euro game.  It is outstanding in its production value, but even more outstanding is Five Tribes' powerful intensity of player interaction.  Player interaction is often mild and sometimes absent in the Euro game genre; near the top of the genre's detractors' list of complaints.  With every move in Five Tribes, a player must carefully consider that his move is not opening up a big scoring opportunity for his opponents.  The mancala mechanic of picking up all the meeples in a space and deploying them in successive adjacent spaces and finally performing the action at the final space reminds me of my favorite game, Trajan.  Five Tribes is an extremely stimulating optimization puzzle shared on the same board with your opponents.  Five Tribes has just the right game length and has a delicious, crunchy, mental puzzle feel.  I almost left out the djinns, which can be summoned by players.  Djinns allow a player to break the rules or score extra points at the end of the game or collect extra money in the game.  The bidding for turn order mechanism works quite well also.  I find the mental challenge of maximizing the points yield of each move very stimulating and enjoyable.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 9
The game is quite brilliant, however it is definitely not without some clear flaws.

My biggest problem with the game is that there isn't enough RNG in the game.  With some abstracts I'm happy for this - sometimes it is nice to really put the brain to work. My problem with Five Tribes is that the ever-changing puzzle in front of you is far too complex.  If you really, REALLY sit there and think, you can often eventually number-crunch a really good play.  In a 4 player game however - this is hugely problematic when 2/4 people are just there to have fun.

I am not s super AP prone person, but I find that in order to try to win, I have to put in at LEAST as much thought as the next AP heavy person, which sort of creates an AP arms race - which is to say it can spin out of control and break the flow of the game.

A game should have its own built-in mechanism to prevent this from happening - the responsibility should not be on the players, because it cheapens the experience.

That said - what is here is AMAZING hence the 9 (and my own selfish bias), but with the wrong group this game is barely worth the time it takes to set it up, and I can think of few games as guilty of this issue as Five Tribes is.

RATING: 4
With potentially 216 opening moves and possibly 1296 variations on those opening moves, the game facilitates analysis paralysis from the outset, and since the board state changes significantly after each players' turn it further encourages down-time as players have to re-evaluate their options creating a more-tactical-than-strategic experience which is then compounded by the bid for turn order mechanism at the start of each round. I like each of the ingredients of this game separately, but when combined they form something unsavory.

I could be convinced to play this game only as a two-player game in the future.

RATING: 8
Bokkensprong 20/02/2015 met Pascal, Sander, Jukebox en ik. Woestijn met 5x6 tegels waarop initieel telkens drie mannetjes in drie kleuren. Elke kleur is een stam met bepaalde eigenschappen (wit: punten en handelswaarde, geel: 2 punten en bonus voor wie er meest heeft, rood: moordenaars die andere kleuren elimineren, blauw: punten voor blauwe tegels, groen: leveren groene kaartjes op die exponentieel punten opleveren). In je beurt neem je alle mannetjes van een bepaalde tegel en volg je daarmee een orthogonaal pad, waarbij je op elke tegel die je over moet een mannetje achterlaat. De kleur van je laatste mannetje moet gelijk zijn aan die van minstens een ander mannetje op je eindtegel. Die mannetjes neem je weg en de eigenschap van die kleur mag je nu gebruiken. Wordt de tegel daarmee leeg, dan mag je een kameel plaatsen op de tegel, die nu van jou wordt. Je mag ook nog de tegelactie uitvoeren, waardoor je kaarten kan krijgen (groene, slaven, geesten (dzjins)). Leuk systeem ook om de beurtvolgorde te bepalen (je kan betalen voor een betere startpositie). Tof, uitdagend spel met toch vrij eenvoudige regels. Niet voor twijfelaars. Geen tekst nodig in het spel, maar wel op de geheugensteunkaarten - helaas enkel in het Engels.
Gekregen voor mijn 40e verjaardag! 24/04/15

RATING: 8
Really cool game. Probably more tactical than strategic but there is definitely room for some broad strategy. I haven't really seen any AP with this game, which is surprising since my group is generally on the AP heavy side of things. It reminds me a bit of a gamer's 7 Wonders somehow. The key I think to enjoying this game is to play a bit by feel, obviously it would be insane to count out every possible legal move and decide which one is best. Generally my perspective is to decide what I want out of a turn and then work backwards from there. So maybe I want some cards, I look for all the spots that let me buy cards and see how to best activate those spots, and I'd also look for spots where I could pick up green guys, etc. The genies are also really cool in that they give you special powers that help focus your play in certain directions. It's far from the deepest game in the world, but it's definitely a serious game and I definitely really enjoy playing it. I think it's the spatial aspect that I really enjoy, although it's unfortunate that outside of the two player game it's rare enough to have two turns in a row that you really can't use one turn to set up a second really strong turn. I imagine I'd love this at two players, unfortunately I rarely play two players.

RATING: 8
Five Tribes is certainly a Euro game, but I understand how the chaotic nature of the game board makes it a game some don't enjoy.

For me, I keep wanting to play it again because the Mancala-like puzzle is addicting. It's one of the few games where I don't focus on the long-game but instead zero in on the best move each turn. Each turn is a satisfying experience and a chance to get better at finding the high-scoring patterns.

My only warning is to not play this with those prone to linger on finding the best move. The game can provide a plethora of choices (especially in the early game), catnip for the "analysis paralysis"-prone player.

RATING: 7
There are some nice ideas in the game, but it cannot really be taken seriously.

Like you can bid for the playing order, but practically only the first player can have any idea what he is bidding for. By the time the second/third player comes, the setup changes dramatically. This is also a problem, because players who bid the lowest can have much better options than the one who bid second.

It is also a problem, because it is an analysis paralysis game by default. But since it changes dramatically by one move, you cannot really use the other player's turn for your own plan.

Nor the tribes neither the Djinns seem to be balanced, but because of the chaos, you can't even imagine that someone would even consider a complete development circle. It is often solved by adding a bidding algorithm, but as I wrote that does not work either.

Still, I understand why it is popular, and because of its uniqueness I would play it again nevertheless.

RATING: 5.5
2014-10-28

Initial Rating: 6.0 (October 2014)

By far the best Days of Wonder game for me. As usual from them, it is gorgeous, but this time they added some depth to match the pretty exterior. Still it is a light-medium weight game for me. Rules are easy to grasp and the playtime is reasonable.

The beginning of the game it is a lot harder because you have so many options, and you are trying to maximize your points without giving away easy plays for your opponents, but after some rounds, the rest of the game becomes much more obvious as there are fewer options each turn. I also really like that you have to give away VPs to decide the play order, that’s a neat mechanic BUT it seems to me that it is only relevant in the last half of the game. At the beginning there are lots of good options.

On the bad side, there can be lots of downtime, at the beginning because there are lots of options to assess and at the end because everyone is really trying to find good plays. Unintentional king making occurs very often in Five Tribes. The use of slaves offended some people in my game group (not me, I really don´t mind this in a game setting). I also dislike the luck inherent in the goods cards and the Djinns, some of them are very unbalanced BUT I’m not sure of this if this is an issue for me. 

Bottom line, it is an ok (mostly) tactical game. I don’t think I will buy it but for now I won’t say no to a game of Five Tribes. I have the feeling the game will go down in my personal ratings with more plays but it is the best DoW game I have ever played.

Current Rating: 6.0

RATING: N/A
[b][url=https://boardgamegeek.com/wiki/page/BoardGameGeek_Golden_Geek#toc1]2014 Golden Geek Winner[/url][/b]

Crossing into the Land of 1001 Nights, your caravan arrives at the fabled Sultanate of Naqala. The old sultan just died and control of Naqala is up for grabs! The oracles foretold of strangers who would maneuver the Five Tribes to gain influence over the legendary city-state. Will you fulfill the prophecy?  Invoke the old Djinns, move the Tribes into position at the right time and the Sultanate may become yours!  Designed by Bruno Cathala, Five Tribes is a bit more of a "Gamers' Game" than Days of Wonder's standard fare.  The game builds on a long tradition of German games with wooden Maples in a unique twist on the Worker Placement game genre. While the rules are quite easy to learn and understand, implementing a winning strategy will take a calculated approach.  In this worker displacement game, you score by carefully maneuvering the Five Tribes of Maples over the tiles that make up the ancient city-state of the Sultanate of Naqala. You need to look ahead to determine what moves will not only position you to score well, but put your opponents at a disadvantage. It's when, how and where you dis-place the Five Tribes of Assassins, Elders, Builders, Merchants & Viziers that will determine your victory or failure!

Players: 2, 3, 4
Time: 60 min
Mechanics: [property=2080][/property], [property=2012][/property], [property=2011][/property], [property=2004][/property]

RATING: 7

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
In Five Tribes, meeples of different colors are randomly distributed across an already randomly distributed grid of 6x5 tiles. A player's turn consists of simply picking up all of the meeples from one tile and dropping them, one by one, across a chain of tiles - and the last tile is required to already have a meeple of the color you are dropping. Each colored meeple represents and different action and you take the action of the last meeple you place by removing that and other meeples from that tile that are of the same color. In this way, the number of the meeples on the board diminishes every turn. If you clear out a tile, you have the option to place one of your camels on it which will score you the points on the tile at the end of the game.

In addition to the meeple action, each tile also has an action that you can optionally perform, so you are constantly balancing the meeple action you want plus the tile action that will most advantage you - while at the same time not setting up your opponents for an obviously killer move. The meeple and tile actions basically feed the many ways there are to score points in this game. When a player has placed all his camels or there are no more legal moves to make on the board, then the game ends. Then you break out the scoring pad and add up points into the hundreds.

The early game feels a little directionless. The possibilities are endless and there are many viable and great moves, which can feel a little pointless. As players start to collect things and preferences start to be defined, things get more interesting. I think what prevents me from scoring this higher is that there are so many ways to score and the points get so high, that it is well nigh impossible to tell how well you are doing, unless one player is really running away with it. It isn't secret end-game scoring, but in some ways it may as well be. The many possible moves could prove daunting for those who like to analyze choices to the nth degree, so you will want to be careful who you play this with. I really enjoyed my play, but was slightly disappointed as we went through the score pad, scoring all of the different possible avenues to points. I had the sense there were too many, and I might prefer if the game were a little more focused. It's a solid title, though, and in my opinion, Cathala's best.

RATING: 9
Days of Wonder has done it again (as has Mr. Cathala).

This is a game that requires perhaps slightly more thought and in-depth tactics/strategies than the typical DoW game, but by no means is it inaccessible.

The modular board and meeple setup, brought together with sound gameplay elements and a production quality that is tops in the industry for art and design make Five Tribes one of, if not THE, best board game of 2014.  

Simply put, it is fun, exciting, thought provoking, and gorgeous to look upon.  

A real winner with staying power and room to go up in rating.

RATING: 7.5
This was one of my favorite new to me games at Geekway. This has a nice Mancala-like mechanic of picking up meeples and dropping one per orthogonal space. The last one dropped needs to match the color of one or more pieces in that space. Pick all of that color up and take a special action based on the color. Then take a special action based on the space. If all the meeples are gone place your camel to own the space. There are many ways to score in the game including owning spaces, collection of one type of meeple, set collection of different resource cards, and buying various genies. The genies also grant special abilities. There were always many options to consider which kept it interesting. I think the game will have a lot of replayability.

RATING: 6
Wood-on-tiles with heavily abstracted spatial relationships and lots of ways to get points. Kind of dependent on everyone counting up scoring opportunities (and their interdependencies) for proper bidding, which also includes knowing which djinn are worth significantly more than their nominal values in the early game, and then re-counting as each action is taken. Even more computational with two since it seems you're planning double moves more often than not.

RATING: 9
Absolutely amazing game. Have played 3 rounds (3 players) the first time and couldn't wait to get back to it when I get more players. I love the amounts of decisions one get to make in this game. And the game seems quite balanced. I've played it 3 different styles (heavy control, heavy builders/viziers, heavy merchant/light control) in those 3 different games and each style played quite well. Perhaps the only nitpick is that some djinns tend to be very situational whereas others may seem overpowered. In one of the games, we also gotten to a point where noone seems to bother to get djinns because of how weak we perceived them to be. Perhaps a potentially way to house-rule this is to discard djinns in play after a couple of rounds or something.

But all in all, Five Tribes... Love it!!!

A lovely and intriguing "points salad" Euro with very little luck involved. If you like Mancala (or good board games), you'll love this.

RATING: 7.3
It feels nicely varied between plays.  Not too heavy but you'll still sit there for 2-3 minutes (at least) for each player's turn as they play out every move they can find in their head.  This can make games at 4 players pretty slow, but if you're willing to sit there and fiddle with your phone while you wait the game is still interesting.  Mancala movement is the primary mode of gameplay.  Having more than 4 meeples on a single tile can break the game pretty easily, so we quickly found it was best to avoid it at all costs.  Genies can break the game pretty easily, and fewer players nerfs the usefulness of the yellow tribe.

Great components and couple of novel ideas, but Five Tribes is more of a puzzle solving contest than a game. Each players' actions change the board completely, so any long term planning is not needed and the game is almost 100% tactical (with a lot of opportunities for AP prone players to freeze the game).

Also, because the board changes so much between players turns the novel player order mechanism doesn't really work - you either want to go first because you spot a good opportunity, or if you didn't spot anything, then it doesn't really matter when your turn is as the board is going to change so much before your go anyways.

RATING: N/A
Five Tribes builds on a long tradition of German-style games that feature wooden meeples. Here, in a unique twist on the now-standard "worker placement" genre, the game begins with the meeples already in place – and players must cleverly maneuver them over the villages, markets, oases, and sacred places tiles that make up Naqala. How, when, and where you dis-place these Five Tribes of Assassins, Elders, Builders, Merchants, and Viziers determine your victory or failure.

RATING: 8
I enjoyed the game Mancala back in the day as a kid. The gameplay seems simple, but each move has 4 layers to it:
What Tribe action do I want?
What board action do I want?
Is this the most beneficial move I can make?
What moves did I just set up for the other players?
This can cause some lag for players that calculate everything.

Its a shame there isn't a rule about clearing the market/Dijinns if no one wants what is available. We had a whole market full of Fakir/slaves and no one wanted to get on that. 

Still, great game and always fun to play. In my top 50
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
In short: I liked it quite the first time I played and I liked it slightly more when played the second time. Yes, it can have problems when played with AP players – unless they also realize they should be playing intuitively in most of the turns. It’s colorful, and the mechanism is way more interesting and unusual than most Eurogames released in the past few years. I had no problem with some djinns being stronger than some other djinns. Also, while I don’t really like it when whatever you do you score points in a game, here it felt it just provided lots of space for creativity. All in all, it’s an enjoyable one, and while it's rather complex for a gateway, it does reeward non-gamer approach to the games.

RATING: 5
Really nice, hefty pieces but the concept looks a lot better on paper than actual play.

A move consists of finding the most points for yourself while wrecking other moves for the next player. It essentially devolves into lots of staring at the table, screening basic math between each person. Lots of downtime here and not much fun.

The map doesn’t scale to player count so the more players the less you get to do (about the same number of moves regardless). With 3 players planning ahead gets pretty worthless and with 4 there’s not enough moves to work with the Djinn powers. Solo actually worked ok. I’ve not yet tried it one-on-one, which sure looks to be the sweet spot.

RATING: 7
I did not care much for the difficulty of planning: the change in the number of meeples on the board can drastically change a plan. My problem with this is that it is hard to predict what the opponents want to play, so it is hard to plan what you will be able to play. Maybe this is remediated by the play order bidding, but still: when you are not early in the turn, it is hard to plan.

I liked the special powers of the djinns. They can be purchased relatively easily, with the right bidding for turn order. However, nothing is guaranteed, and I did not like the feeling of being denied a piece of a plan I had investigated in creating, when someone snatches the djinn I wanted.

I am also not a fan of the point salad: I have a hard time even starting to reason about the various ways of making points.

The setup is also quite long compared to the game.

Not bad, but Istanbul (played just before) feels so much cleaner and interesting (with more control).

RATING: 7.5
Can't decide how I feel about this game. I guess my first thought when it comes to this game is "fiddly". RATING: 8.5

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
First impressions: Don't play this game if you have a lot of AP-prone people, as the huge amount of possible turn options in addition to the fact that the board state can change pretty dramatically from turn to turn makes this a nightmare for those people. This game has a pretty cool take on the mancala mechanic, where which colored meeple you set down as you weave your path, and also how you weave your path has subtle optimizations. Also, that the tiles themselves have actions adds an additional factor that you have to think about when making your move. 

My cons of this game are 1. the game is a bit too much of point salad without too much thematic backing (e.g. why do the yellow meeples score more points? Why can you put 5 buildings on one spot, and why do they only give points? Similarly the goods are basically just points), 2. I thought the djinn would play a more central role in the game but it is hard to get a ton of them unless you specifically dedicate yourself to do so, and only a few seem to have game-changing effects unless you completely dedicate yourself to utilizing their abilities, and 3. that there are more options in the beginning which gradually dwindles, when it would be better if there were more options later when everyone else knew what main point scoring mechanism they are pursuing.

Components-wise this game has a ton of large, chunky wooden pieces, and you really are getting your money's worth. The art on the djinn is fantastic although the art on the goods and the tiles are just ok.

EDIT: I've come to like this game less because outside of its mancala mechanism, it feels very point-salady/doesn't do a very good job of hiding the fact you are just doing various stuff for points. Probably has to do with the fact I've played more Euros too so it doesn't feel as unique.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 4
This game was pretty boring to play.  I knew going into it that it wasn't going to be for me but I wanted to give it a chance.  I confirmed my suspicions. 

The main issue is you can't plan ahead. The board will most likely change before you are able to make your move.  You will have to do your full move analysis on your turn which means everyone will be waiting for you and you for them.  AP people beware!  In most games you can plan ahead and then execute your turn when it is time. In this game you have to do all the analysis during your turn.  This slow the game down and means that most of the game you are sitting around doing absolutely nothing because it is pointless to do any kind of analysis beforehand.

Also, it is extremely frustrating when you really want to land on a specific tile but none of the nearby tiles will work so that you have enough guys to land on it.  It just doesn't feel satisfying... especially since you burn a lot of analysis/brain power on trying to figure out how you can possibly activate that square and none of the combinations work.  I don't have AP but I certainly felt like I did while playing this game.  People in our group were frequently apologizing because this game turns everyone into an AP player and we all felt bad for taking so long on our turns because of the mechanics in this game. 

100% tactical game.  Strategy does not pay off.  Blehhhhh.....

J'ai tardé à m'y lancer, car les explications de l'auteur en vidéo m'avait donné l'impression d'une grosse usine à gaz. Je regrette juste que l'interaction ne soit pas plus prononcée (moins subtile) entre les joueurs. J'ai également beaucoup apprécié le côté opportuniste du jeu, qui m'a rappelé un petit peu "Euphrat & Tigris". Bruno Cathala est décidément toujours un auteur à suivre de très très près.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 10
wonderful game with nice mechanics and awesome material !
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
Days of Wonder "gamers game" but has a very intuitive feel once you start play.  You pick up meeples and "mandala style" drop them one at a time in other spaces.  You place a same colored meeple in another space and that ability activates.  You then resolve the space as well.  If it is the last meeple of that color, you claim the space.  

There are cards with special abilities and many different paths to victory.  It is a very good game and I really liked it. 

Very AP prone players ought to stay away.  It will run long with a lot of AP.

RATING: N/A
Auf dem Spielplan sind viele Orte zu finden. Dort befinden sich anfangs zufällig platzierte Spielfiguren aus fünf verschiedenen Farben – den Five Tribes. Ist ein Spieler am Zug, nimmt er alle Spielfiguren von einem Ort und verteilt sie neu. Die letzte Figur darf nur auf einem Feld platziert werden, auf dem bereits wenigstens eine Figur in der entsprechenden Farbe steht. 
Für den Spieler entscheidend sind die Farbe der letzten Figur und das Zielfeld. Denn beides bestimmt nun die eigentlichen Aktionsmöglichkeiten.
Nun entfernt der Spieler alle Spielfiguren auf dem Zielfeld in der entsprechenden Farbe der zuletzt gesetzten Figur und nutzt deren Vorteile für sich.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7.75
Great game with lots of strategy - the board is comprised of 25 unique tiles which are place in a 5 by 5 random grid which already makes for variety. each tile starts with an assortment of different colored meeples. your turn is to pick a tile then pick up all meeples on that tile and then you move by dropping off a meeple to an adjacent tile and keep going (no doubling back) till you have no meeples to drop. you get the action on the last tile you drop and a bonus if you can clear the tile (droping a meeple and there is only 1 color of meeples on that tile)
haven't played this enuff - its a definite keeper

RATING: 9.9
IMHO one of the best 2p games ever made.
This game deserves a 9,9 as a 2p game while it doesn't even reach a 6 if you play it as a 4p.
Not only because of the inevitable analysis-paralysis that will leave you with enough time to smoke a joint in between players' turns but because you can't even try to plan ahead since the board state will change a lot before you'll play again.

As a 2p game it is a whole different game, which requires you to play tactically in order to adapt to your opponent's strategy while trying to force your own.

RATING: 8
2017 Top 100 Favorites of All-Time: #46

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
Awesome in a box. Such a cool game. The components, the art, the theme, the play time, the decisions, the insert, the mancala...it's just so cool! I can see why Five Tribes was a smash hit this year. It plays really well with 2, despite Richard Ham's complaints. I will say that 60 minutes is probably not feasible for this game, even with 2, unless you have several games under your belt. Expect 90 minutes with 2 your first couple plays (120 minutes with 4). There are a lot of decisions in this game, and they don't usually appear as obvious. My wife had some crazy AP going on when we played. To be honest, that is my only complaint. If you notice any AP in your game group, it will certainly manifest here. If not for the AP, this game might be a 10 or close to it. As it stands, it's still quite excellent. 

Update 1/31/15: 

Erin and I played again tonight. It ended quite differently, to my dismay. With the help of a useful Djinn card, she burned through her camels crazy fast, and ended the game abruptly. I was only halfway into my strategy when she said "okay, it's over." To be honest, I was pretty upset by it, considering I had a pretty good synergy going. On the other hand, I liked how the game ended so differently. There's a lot going on here, and it seems clear so far, that this is just a great game!
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
I was concerned with the Analysis Paralysis, but after playing it it's actually not that bad. There's just too many potential choices to be able to math out each one, just accept that you don't have the time or patience to do so. Just pick one you like and roll with it. I love the asymmetrical sandbox gameplay, you're not forced into any particular action, you can just choose whatever you like to do or focus on. And the worker displacement mechanic is incredible.

It's another solid medium euro from Days of Wonder. From heavier side like the ones they've put out last couple of years (Relic Runners, Cargo Noir..). But sadly it's still not a hit like they used to make.. I think biggest problems with this one are that when you become good with the mancala mechanic it will be very chess-like (ok, not quite, there' good randomness too). Second problem relates to first one and it's that Five Tribes is pretty abstract and dry game. I prefer Trajan mancala to this one, and when even Stefan Feld game is more FUN than DoW game, something is wrong! Still, the components are very high quality, auction mechanic is nice addition for interaction and like I said it's a solid game. It's better than average euros, but from DoW I expect more! 

update: I've come to like this bit more. This game invites you to take itself too seriously, and I never would've thought, but I was the one to fall to that trap. When you let go a little (don't think of every consequence of your actions) and stop trying too hard, it can be a fun game. 7->8

2-Player Comment:
* There's nice 2p variant in the box where you use 2x betting tokens each. It's a nice touch from DoW. 2p game is bit more hardcore than 3-4, but works very well.

RATING: 9
Don't have much experience with the mancala mechanic, but I enjoyed it a lot in this game. Lots of ways to get points, and always interesting to see which is the best path available. 

I wouldn't say the slave cards bother me a lot , but I do wonder why they had to be included. When we play, we talk about freeing the slaves when using these cards, like some have suggested here on BGG. 

The concern for "AP" is there, and the ability to plan ahead is limited by the ever-changing board. But my group seems to be able to keep each game of this moving without too much delay. And I do think you stay engaged during other players' turns, to see what might open upon the board.

I've heard the criticism that the end can be anti-climatic, where there are only a few legal moves to make, so the group walks each player through what is available to him, but I myself don't find this a huge issue.

RATING: 10
I love the "Mancala" core mechanic of this game, with thoughtful strategy regarding where you want your last meeple to land (are there any singles in sight to assassinate and claim two territories?) and how valuable the First Turn marker is to you in the next round.  I've played with my core gaming group, with my work group (who gravitate more towards gateway games), and at the local Beers & Board Games night.  It was a hit in all of the groups.  Very unique game play compared to anything else on the market.  I can't get enough of it!  Also, although I haven't tried it yet, I believe the 2 player variant would be just as much fun as having 3 or 4 players.

RATING: 9
I don't think of this as a point salad, necessarily, because you can win by getting points from each category or by specializing.

Not a point salad - I won my last game without getting a single card and with only three camels on the board. I had three Djinns that paid off huge. One doubled my only builder move of the game, one boosted my viziers to 3 points each (of which I had a plurality), and one allowed me to get free Djinns (got this one first). So, on every turn but one I went for yellows and whites.

Point salad - In the previous game, I was middling in each category and scraped by with a win. I had 7 unique cards, took second place in viziers, made a couple of strong building moves, had five camels out, and had two merely okay Djinns. I didn't bid high at all in this game, so essentially I won because my points per turn were consistently solid even though I never made a particularly big move.

RATING: 3
11/25/14 Played a four-player game in 65 minutes. 
The vague "1001 Nights" theme made no sense to me at all. Apparently each of the five meeple colors was a tribe. Though I have no idea how there can be whole tribes of Viziers (yellow meeples) or Elders (white meeples). So three minutes into the explanation I knew I had a turkey on my hands, and... it just got worse. The Djinn cards - almost every one had hard to decipher icons on them. So every time a new one was revealed I had to ask, "What does that mean?" After a while I just quit asking. I no longer cared what happened in this game; I just wanted it to be over. I stopped agonizing over my turns after about the fourth one and just did any old thing that was legal the rest of the game, to make to game take as little time as possible. I knew I'd lose and I didn't care. I'd scoop up a handful of meeples and spread them over the board, not caring if they made me any points or not.  
Five tribes is indeed a "point salad" type game with what seems like random game mechanics bolted together in a miasma of arbitrary rules. The phrase "the theme is pasted on" is raised to new heights here. The *huge* potential for analysis paralysis is only superceded by the amount of chaos, as planning ahead is virtually impossible. 
Five Tribes was a total waste of time, that I hope to never see again.

RATING: 4
Mancala with special abilities and a grid! Every turn feels very much like its own puzzle - though especially with the Djinns you sometimes play for a long time investment.

I like more direct screwage, and while this one can bring it - I think it takes a bit too much to get there as compared to something like Patchwork - thus this feels like a bit like Kingdom Builder except there I feel that you have more of a "strategy" to play towards. Anyway, this is a fun puzzle, the heuristics for parsing the board state are tricky but once you realize that depending on odd or even number of meeples from the origin tile you can ignore half the tiles.

A good game!

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
This is probably the best Euro I have played. Unlike most Euros, Five Tribes has variable strategies that you can pursue, and no one strategy is particularly better than the other and there is no "optimal route" for you to min max out. Further, each move every player takes changes your options dynamically, making it difficult to do that elusive "best play" that characterizes the winning strategies of most Euros. The one strategy that may be strongest is a Blue money strategy, but it's hard to setup and easy to block if your opponent knows what he is doing, or he can take it from you himself! So it really works out well.

It also helps that the game plays like Sungka, which is a simple game from my childhood. And I love the theme -- this game promotes slavery, human sacrifice, murder, coup d'etas and demon worship. How awesome is that?? The only Euro I have ever rated a 9.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 4
Pretty much exactly what I expected. I enjoy the ingredients of this point salad. The djinn cards are just the right level of super power. But my favorite part is probably the game's biggest downfall: the puzzly action selection mechanism. I enjoy decisions that have multiple ramifications and tradeoffs. A single move here can be motivated by one or more of: the tile action, tile control, the tribal action, opportunity removal, setting up future moves (risky), plus compounding any of the above with bonus scoring/benefits. Unfortunately with the combinatorial explosion of possible moves, players can really get bogged down in finding the optimal move, then deciding between equivalent moves based on which hurts your opponents most, then ensuring you aren't setting someone else up. And planning ahead is difficult because so much changes between turns. Even if the move you have your eye on is still there when your turn comes around, maybe a better one has surfaced? Better check. Personally I'm not willing to put more than 20s into a play which can have a detrimental effect on my score, which is fine, but likely also sets up moves for whoever follows a la Puerto Rico. This is further compounded because the player that follows likely paid less than I did for the opportunity. This doesn't kill the fun for me, but I can see players being incompatible when it comes to this game: some play too slow, some play too "randomly," whatever. I enjoy this one but I'll be picky who I play with and I don't need to own it. But it does earn the distinction of my favorite Cathala game!

UPDATE: Abyss has taken the "favorite Cathala" prize. Further, I've lost interest in playing this game again. The AP, the puzzle, taking what's there rather than what you want, it's just not fun after the novelty of the puzzle wears off.

Now, I will admit that this game definitely favors the player that knows the game's cadence above a player that is playing for the first time, but there are very few games I know of that bring newbies in the same level as experienced players--especially games with this amount of depth. Five Tribes truly shines, in my opinion, in all categories of awesome.

RATING: 9.3
Five Tribes is a beautifully produced mancala game.  It's a fast-paced abstract game with a very broad decision space.  I think that after the first few turns, you should hit on a strategic focus that guides the rest of your game.  You then maximally exploit that strategy each turn while limiting your opponent's ability to do the same.  It's a great blending of strategy and tactics.

One of the tricky things is that no strategy is so dominant that you can ignore everything else while pursuing it.  You need to be best in two or three point-scoring categories and close in the others.  This means you need to watch what's happening very closely and make sure you are not falling too far behind in any category. If you are: you need to find a way to catch up in that category.  This is an ever-present tension in the game. You find yourself feeling good about your game with regards to viziers until you realize that you have completely ignored the resource market & you think your opponent might have a set of 9 different resources!

RATING: 7
Surprisingly simple but deep game. Grab workers from one tile and drop them on adjacent tiles until you end up on the tile that is most profitable. There are many different paths to earn victory points but they are all fairly easy to evaluate so the tactical decision never turns into analysis paralysis. Claim tiles, develop tiles, trade goods, acquire djinns or perhaps assassin someone - each decision is yours and each decision is fun!

RATING: 7

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7.5
Based on the Tales of 1001 nights, this game designed by Bruno Cathala has a 6 by 5 set of tiles seeded with three meeples each on the tiles. There are five different colors of meeples. Players then move the meeples, mancala style. For instance, if there are 3 meeples on a tile, you have to move 3 hexes, dropping off a meeple at each hex. Where you end your move, there must be a matching colored meeple to the last meeple, and you get to remove those meeples: Yellow meeples count for majority scoring, while meeples allow you to purchase bonus cards call Djinns, green meeples allow you to collect cards for set collecting VPS, red meeples allow you to assasinate other meeples and blue meeples score for adjacent blue numbers as well. In addition, each tile has a special ability (buy cards, buy Djinns, place palace/tree) that you execute when you finish your turn.

Each turn, you also bid for turn order on a fixed track, so you can always bid $0 if you do not wish to spend VPs. 

This is a really engaging game. It can get very complex, since this game is more about not leaving behind valuable points after your turn, yet there are a few tactical possibilities. This could be Cathala's best game and I'm looking forward to more plays. I keep thinking about what I want to do next game.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
I've only had the chance to play a couple of times but I think I love this game. It felt unlike anything I've played before. 

I've heard people complain that it can give people a case of Analysis Paralysis, however I felt that it gets over that by overwhelming the player with so many options, particularly at the start, that it's clear that no effective analysis can be achieved within a reasonable time, so you just have to pretty much wing it. That feeling of winging it, of trying to navigate through the possibility space on instinct, felt exactly right for me. That does mean that the game only works if no-one takes it seriously enough to try and play perfectly, but when you play with the right people it works wonderfully.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7.3
I like the look and feel of this as a euro even though I wish the board looked somehow less abstracted. The abundance of meeples and the ease of set up despite their sheer number is a huge plus. This game seems like a good point salad game that will have a lot of replayability and appeal to Euro gamers especially or those in that mood. I like the different types of meeples though I do really wish each different colored meeple had a different shape to reflect its occupation. That really would have took away some abstraction and improved the feel of the game. The overall system, mechanics, and strategy are strong and decisive. The game uses a lot of future thinking/visualization and spacial reasoning: two things I don't excel at so those could hurt my feeling towards the game. This is definitely a game I would enjoy playing  a game and feel that like 7 wonders, it likely has an enjoyment curb (not a learning curb but it takes a few games before it gets really fun--this is common with non-light euros I think)

This is not one I would own but would enjoy playing and recommend to those who like a good euro game. It's possible it was over hyped but that's hardly a new phenomenon for any time of new thing that a lot of people like.

One thing I don't like is that because the board will be slightly tweaked right before your turn (the last player will alter the board a little changing your options) you therefore have to do all your decision making on your turn and can only do SOME planning on other people's turns. And because the game is all about future thinking and planning multiple turns ahead in a complex interactive system, it can lend itself to analysis paralysis in a way that is similar to chess and isn't terribly exciting. The slower you take your turn and the more sluggishly you play, the better you'll probably do. I like it when games have some mitigating factors that take away incentive to really drag out your turn and this game lacks it.

Despite that critique, I think it's an excellent and awesome game and as long you don't mind AP (analysis paralysis) or you're playing with players who are not prone to it, it should be fine.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 7.8
An extremely variable set-up (there are randomized tiles, meeples, genies, and cards) ensures that no two games of this will likely play the same. The bidding mechanism *seems* intelligently designed, because it will allow players to weigh their potential gains versus potential costs. What's curious about it, though, is that the board can radically change from one player's move to the next, so a player who pays for 2nd place might not have the options they expected available. It will take more plays to see how impactful this element of the design can be. That aside, this is a clever, subtly innovative game that seems mind-boggling at first and then like second nature within a play or two.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

I have enjoyed this game every time I have played it, and I have walked away wanting to play again.

RATING: 7.5
I like it quite a bit, but many of the qualities I view as strengths could also be construed as weaknesses.

Nice mid-weight game that has a good amount of things happening at any one time, but isn't hard to explain for new players. However, the game sits at a slightly awkward spot where I think it's a bit too complex for new gamers, but not quite heavy enough for the more experience people in my circles.

I enjoy the feeling of picking up and dropping meeples across the board, and how that movement constantly changes the options available so that you need to stay flexible in pursuing your goals. However, that same movement makes it hard to plan ahead because a meeple dropped in the wrong spot will ruin any plans you've made while waiting for your turn to come around.

RATING: 7
Typical of Days of Wonder productions, Five Tribes has beautiful artwork, good components, a great box insert, and easy-to-understand gameplay.

Unfortunately, despite the relative simplicity its of play, turns involve processing a lot of variables, so FT suffers from a high rate of analysis paralysis. Also, endgame scoring involves a points tally that runs high, meaning it will most likely require a calculator to compute.

A good game, but too much waiting, both for turns and endgame scoring, was a deal breaker for me.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
Mancala?  What the heck IS Mancala?  Pick up everyone at the square you are in, and drop one in each square orthogonal to yours until you land at a square you want to be THAT HAS A WORKER OF THE SAME COLOR AS ONE IN YOUR HAND.

Depending on that color, you get to do an ability.  

I did not enjoy this game with four players, the board just changes drastically from turn to turn and you don't feel like there is any real strategy in finding a combination, you just look at the board state when it IS your turn, and hope there is something good/left for you.

I thought this game SHINED in two player.  This way you can set yourself up for TWO turns in a row and actually set up a pretty cool combination that allows you to get a BIG score.

RATING: 7.5

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6.5
This was a game I was very interested in. I heard raving reviews and saw wonderful images about its art and components. Truly, a Day of Wonder production. I have to say though that I found the terrain tiles a bit dull, and the demons art doesn't match very well with the rest.

The rules can be explained in about 20 minutes: you basically move groups of meeples, but you have to leave one per tile you cross. The final tile determines one of your 2 actions; the colour of the last meeple sets the second.

Then there are many ways to score, "a la Feld", but they are totally inconnex (a bit like "Bora Bora"), with several mechanisms (mayories, set collection, etc.). Some of them are somewhat abstract: it's difficult to know exactly how many points you have won with this action.

The theme is completely pasted. You never get to believe it, not even at the beginning. 

The big issue with this game is that it is very slow. I won't say it's AP prone (but at least it's arguable). There are many possible moves and at least you need to invest some time to check some. Even the initiative bid can feel lenghtly.

It's not a bad game, but besides the meeple movement mechanic, there's nothing really new/original to it, and it really drags. It would improve a lot if it could be played in 30-40 minutes.

I would play again, but I don't think it's a game I would want to play more than just a few times.

I was certainly dissapointed. For me, it didn't live up to the hype :'(
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
Insane amount of constant analysis paralysis:
- First there is collective AP when everyone is searching for the most profitable move.
- Then there is AP for the turn order bid.
- At the start of a player's turn, there's the AP for the optimal move again, because somebody overbid you for turn order and already took the move you wanted.
- Finally, there's AP during a turn, about not wanting to leave easy scoring options for the next player.

RATING: 3
Nope, nope, nope.  After three plays I can say I'm done with this game.  Experienced gamers can get bogged down with analysis paralysis, because you're not only thinking about your ideal move, but about what it sets up for the next player.  Inexperienced players, on the other hand, can easily set up ridiculous moves for the next player.  Worst of all is playing with a mix of the two -- I mean, who doesn't want to be told "if you did this, you would have gotten 15 more points" by another player after they make their move?  It would be difficult to convince me to play this again.

RATING: 10
This very quickly became an absolute favorite. 

I think it is amazing as a 2 player game. Yes, back-to-back turn are much more common and even more is possible, but it's part of the game to make people pay a lot for getting them. Back-to-back turns also allow for nice combinations where you can set yourself up, which to us does not even increase the time a turn takes as you have to look for what you wanna do and what you leave behind is a question for every turn not just back-to-back turns. 

Yes, I can see some people having AP problems here, but we don't have them much. Due to the game being very abstract with all information open, one can always study the board while it's another persons turn and even though it may change a lot when others took there turn, it's something that keeps us very interested and which gives already a good "feeling" of the state of the board. Especially certain Dschinns also give you a direction for a more long term strategy, which makes finding good moves a little more "directed". From time to time a turn can last a little longer, but in this game we just don't have a big issue with this. 

It also does have a learning curve to it. After a couple of games, certain "patterns" for good moves became more obvious for us, reducing playtime quiet a bit. 

I also love the fact that one can go very different strategies and still end up with a close result. I'm not always a fan of point salads, but here for me it just works, because it is tied together with this really great mancala mechanic.

You either spend a few dollars and go first and second, or save a few dollars and go third and fourth. With more players, it would be far more interesting. All based off of one play at 2 players, so keep that in mind.

RATING: 8
Dans la lignée d'Abyss avec un côté plus stratégique peut-être... Il s'agit du même auteur en même temps et ça se sent (on peut faire le parallèle entre les peuples et les tribus notamment).
Five Tribes est bien car il est possible de "miser" des points de victoire pour être peut-être le premier à jouer le prochain tour. Cette mécanique apporte un bon souffle au jeu car chaque tour est différent.
On peut lui reprocher à 4 joueurs :
 - d'être un peu chaotique : si on joue en dernier, toute stratégie sera irrémédiablement avortée par les autres joueurs qui jouent avant.
 - de s'ennuier car impossible de réfléchir à ce que l'on pourra à faire plus tard (voir ci-dessus).

Ce que j'aime dans ce jeu c'est qu'il n'y a pas de méthode miracle pour gagner à part faire un peu de tout avec ce qu'on a, le jeu étant bien équilibré. Si on délaisse une source de points de victoire, le score s'en partira sûrement. Le tout est donc de savoir quoi faire au bon moment. De l'opportunisme pur et dur donc :-).
Les interactions entre joueurs sont assez minimales mais suffisantes pour pimenter un minimum les parties.
Un bon jeu qui deviendra assurément un classique.

RATING: 7
心得:
算是棋類遊戲，利用撥棋概念配和米寶跟版圖能力組合起來的遊戲
真的要贏可能要算很多
但是競標順位又顯得有點麻煩
是屬於認真玩 等待時間會非常長的遊戲
規則:
*3/4人，一個顏色的8駱駝和1回合標記，2人時各拿藍色或粉紅的11駱駝與2回合標記，每位玩家拿取9個5元和5個1元，面向下
*將板塊隨機排成5x6的方陣，將米寶從袋中隨機抽取，每個板塊3個，將資源卡洗牌，翻開9張，將精靈卡洗牌，翻開3張
*結束時，1.每1元金幣1分 2.黃色米寶1分，每有1位玩家黃色米寶比你少額外10分 3.每個白色米寶2分 4.所有精靈分數加總 5.所有你的板塊分數加總，棕梠樹3分，宮殿5分，每組商品的分數。
*在每次回合一開始時，所有玩家必須競標該回合的行動順序，由拍賣軌上1的玩家開始，將回合標記放到順序軌上的空格，並支付該格子的金額，其他玩家依序放置並支付金額，多位玩家競標0的位置，先出價的玩家順位在後，將該玩家的回合標記往後移動，由出價高的玩家優先在回合執行行動。
*玩家依據順序軌的順序執行回合行動
1.移動回合標記 將順序軌上自己的回合標記移動到競標軌的第一個空格
2.移動米寶 拿取選擇板塊上的米寶，移動到相鄰的板塊，每移動一格，就放下1個米寶，直到所有米寶都放置完畢
>放置的最後一個，必須與最後板塊上的一個顏色相同
>不可以斜線移動
>移動後不可以立刻往回走，但可以在同一回合內繞一圈回到走過的板塊。
>在規劃移動時將米寶放倒，等到確定路線後再讓米寶站起來，以免忘記無法回溯。
3.確認板塊控制權 完成移動時，將你最後一個和板塊上所有同色移除，如果清空，放置1個駱駝在該板塊上
>在玩家移動時，可以經過或停留在自己或別人控制的板塊。
>駱駝一旦放置，控制權就不會被移除，板塊的控制權不會易主
4.部落行動 執行最後一個放置米寶的部落行動
>黃色官員部落 得到板塊上的所有黃色米寶
>白色長老部落 得到板塊上的所有白色米寶
>綠色商人部落 米寶放回袋中，從最前方開始拿取等數量翻開的資源卡，先不要從牌庫補牌。
>藍色建築師部落 米寶放回袋中，然後將米寶的數量乘以九宮格內藍色標記板塊的數量，獲得金幣，可以棄掉奴隸卡，1張奴隸卡藍色米寶數量+1。
>紅色刺客部落 米寶放回袋中，然後殺掉1個其他米寶，移除的紅色米寶數就是暗殺範圍，可以選擇殺1個對手白色或黃色米寶，每棄掉1張奴隸卡範圍+1，暗殺後清空板塊可放置1駱駝在該板塊上，如果只剩1個駱駝，必須優先控制移動得到的板塊，被暗殺的米寶放入袋中。
5.板塊行動 執行最後停留板塊的板塊行動(無論玩家是否控制該板塊)，有紅色箭頭，代表強制行動，一定要執行
>綠洲 放置1棕梠樹，如果沒有棕梠樹，直接忽略此行動。
>村落 放置1城堡，如果沒有棕梠樹，直接忽略此行動
>小市場 支付3元，從翻開的資源卡前3張挑選1張，先不要補牌
>大市場 支付6元，從翻開的資源卡前6張挑選2張，只剩下1張還是要支付6元，先不要補牌
>聖地 玩家支付2白色米寶或1白色米寶+1奴隸卡，從翻開的精靈卡選取1張，將支付的長老放回袋中，奴隸卡棄牌，先不要捕牌。
*精靈有特殊能力，一些特殊能力需要支付花費，需支付花費的能力只能在自己的回合執行，且1回合1次，拿到精靈後就可以立刻執行精靈能力
6.商品販售(選擇性行動) 回合結束前可以選擇是否販賣1或多張商品卡，如果販賣多張，每張商品卡的種類都要不，依據販賣的張數得到對應的金幣
*重整 當所有玩家都執行完回合後，補牌>，資源卡往前移動填補空位，然後從牌庫翻牌，補滿9張>補滿3張精靈卡，如果牌庫耗盡，將棄牌重新洗牌形成新的牌庫
*當回合結束，發生下列兩種狀況時遊戲停止
1.當一位玩家將最後一隻駱駝放到板塊上後，待所有玩家完成相同數量的回合，遊戲結束開始計分。
2.沒有任何米寶可以合法移動時，玩家的回合不能再進行移動，但可以選擇是否執行精靈的行動，待所有玩家完成相同數量的回合，遊戲結束開始計分。

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
A multiplayer abstract with no hidden information that channels Mancala. If anything speaks to analysis paralyis and the potential for kingmaking that's it, and it is. The kingmaking is obfuscated by having about 8 different ways to score thankfully - or not thankfully if you're not a fan of point salads. It's a game that rewards specialisation over generalisation though, but it's not easy to specialise. The perennial question each turn is whether I can effectively progress in my specialisation, or are there tactical plays that are rich in points, and is either or both setting up an opponent for a big play, and do I need to take a lesser play to deny an opponent a big play. Given there's 30 tiles on the board to move meeples around, that's a lot of analysis to undertake each turn. The other major downside is that there's no story arc -  the decision process you take in a turn at the end of the game is fundamentally the same as what you do in a turn at the start - assess the board, do the best you can. There's no personal board development, no sense of personal progression, no personal location relevance - just grab points as they come with the board that's presented to you. While each turn provided an engaging decision which would normally warrant a higher rating, running down the same unvarying decision tree each turn became more problematic as the game developed. The lack of theme meant it wasn't saveable.

RATING: 8
Solid game.  The mancala mechanic has been used before but there are elements of the game that feel fresh.  There are many ways to gain points, so it is somewhat of a point salad.  The propensity for AP may be overhyped as there are opportunities to loosely follow a strategy hence you tend to hone in on certain moves.  Also, as meeples start coming off the board, there are fewer tiles to examine.  Art and components are through the roof and for me, the fun factor is quite high.  Also, because the meeples are distributed randomly, the tiles are arranged randomly, and the djinn cards are not all used, the replay value should be high.

The only negative I see is the scoring may be a bit too diverse.  It is very difficult to judge midgame who is most likely winning.

  And contrary to what some may say, I think it is actually great  with 2.

RATING: 7
This is an excellent game. It works as a great bridge from gateway games into meatier ones, without being too heavy. 

I love how literally everything is worth points in the game. Even the money is worth points at a one to one ratio, but you use the money to buy everything else. 

Once you've played a few times, the game time drops significantly, and it's a great game to play between heavier euros as a palate cleanser. 

The component quality is excellent, as you would expect from Days of Wonder. The cardboard chits are thick stock, the colours are vibrant. The meeples have little turbans if you look closely. 

All in all, a wonderful game.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
Five Tribes is a very abstract game with a mancala-like mechanism (which makes it seem more like a Rudiger Dorn game than a Bruno Cathala game). Each turn, players select a tile with a number of tokens on it, and "travel" along the board with those tokens, leaving one on each square, until they reach the final square, at which they get to perform some action depending on the color of the token and the symbol on the tile. There are multiple paths to victory, but much of the game involves staring at the board and trying to find the best move (I worry about AP here). The turn order bidding seems like a trap, too--if you don't win first, the game state will probably be changed enough that you need to re-evaluate everything.

(1 play)

RATING: 5
Five Tribes got so much hype after Gen-Con. It’s easy to see why, with the lavish production synonymous with a Day’s of Wonder game, and the promise of more depth than their other productions. While there are a lot of interesting things going on, I found in a four-player game that planning was too difficult, which made the experience much more tactical than I would have liked. 

I really enjoyed the mancala-like mechanism for action selection, buying Djinns that grant special powers, multiple paths to score big points, and bidding points on the turn-order track. On the other hand, unless I know I’m going to go first in turn order (and to guarantee this costs a ridiculous number of points), I cannot predict what I’ll be able to do on my turn. The board changes so much with each player’s move that any plans I might have had could be ruined (even with multiple contingencies). I could live with that if players were intentionally blocking me, but the act of picking up and putting down meeples in a path can lead to really arbitrary choices by the acting player that severely influence everyone else. 

Overall, the things I did like I liked enough that I’m willing to try again, but this definitely won’t see space on my shelf for the foreseeable future.

RATING: 9
This game is incredibly satisfying to play. It's tense and relaxing at the same time. It suffers from offering so many decisions, but the options are all fantastic choices to have to choose from. With money as points, the turn order bid has a huge impact. If you afford yourself the time needed to play plus some extra -- and play while having brunch where you can give the current player all the time they want to crunch through the many options while you go refill your mimosa -- you're in for a good time.

Quick gameplay once everybody figures out the mechanic.

RATING: 7
This is a really interesting game where you pick up a pile of workers, and drop them one at a time along the tiles forming a path to your final tile where you resolve your actions. You get an effect based on the color meeple you dropped and an additional effect from the tile itself. There are a number of different and interesting point scoring categories, which allow you to explore different strategies. There are lots of imaginative djinn cards with interesting abilities to choose from as well, all worth varying victory points.

Overall a nice game. I liked how many good options the game gives you, I liked the tactical play of trying to not set your opponent up for too good a move on their turn despite the futility of trying. The components are nice. I just couldn't get myself to feel passionately about it. There's nothing wrong with it, and maybe one day I'll miss having it around, but I feel like I'm fine not having the box sit on my shelf collecting dust. Maybe the actions are too equitable. Maybe the lack of any real engine building is what fails to light my fire.

:star: Art, Iconography and components functional
:star: Clever or interesting mechanisms
:nostar: Reasonably low downtime between turns with mildly AP players
:star: Multiple plays stay interesting
:star: Low or mitigatable luck elements
:star::star::star::nostar::nostar: Raw enjoyment

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 5
I'll start with some things I liked about Five Tribes. All the action happens on a shared board, and as a consequence it's pretty interactive. It's not overly fiddly either.

But it seems to me to essentially come down to a series of puzzles to solve, each one mostly independent of the next. Sure, you can have something of a strategy, but the game is overwhelmingly tactical and the moves are far too easy to evaluate purely in terms of immediate VP gain rather than more interesting trade-offs.

And oh, the downtime if your players are overanalytical! There's a turn-order auction at the start of each round, but only the high-bidder really knows what they're paying for, because the board will have changed for the others. The game also has an odd structure of starting with an overwhelming number of options and then gradually removing them until none remain. As a result, it seemed rarely worth bidding anything for turn order until the mid-game; just take whatever's left when it gets to your turn. 

The scoring system is also a bland 7-Wonders-like spreadsheet. A bit of area majority, a bit of set collection, season to taste. It's too bad, because the underlying mancala mechanism isn't bad but it gets smothered by everything else. Our feeling was that you could play slowly, really deliberating over the details of each move, or you could play badly, leaving huge opportunities for the next player. Neither is that appealing.

RATING: 3
I liked the mancala meeple movement mechanic to claim the action and score depending on the type of meeple being removed. 
There are several types of scoring, probably too many. Blue score based on neighboring tiles, Green to jump start card set collections. Red to remove meeples to allow you to score additional tiles. Yellow straight up scoring, white to build your engine. 
The tiles allow you to build up the scoring potential of the tiles (palm trees/palaces), buy genies that allow you modify the base scoring, buy set cards...

People criticized Tikal for AP. It doesn't hold a candle compared to Five Tribes. In Tikal AP is tolerated, in Five Tribes it is demanded.

With board full at the beginning and so many options available, there is a ridiculous amount of analysis.
Everyone has to study the board to decide what to bid.
Restudy how things had changed if they weren't first when it their turn.
Restudy yet again when placing meeples so they don't open up something too generous for the next player in line.
All of which made it a tedious game. I didn't mention the time it takes to calculate the final scores.

Or just ignore it all start with any old tile and make a guess.

Let's just say after playing it few times I don't need a any more experiences.

RATING: 10
Very clever game, everything is open, you try to optimize your strategy (often relying on an early Djinn acquisition) and it feels pleasant.

Not too many "in your face" moves and only a little player interaction through bids for turn order and obtaining djinns/items before others.

Works well with two with very combo-ish turns (you can have "double" turns).

This game is very prone to AP, I tend to recommend to analyze the board during other people's turn trying to find your best chance to earn VP's and when it's your turn, you only have to calculate how not to give too many options away.

A very refined game with several paths to victory (although you cannot neglect any).
