RATING: 8
2016 RANK: OVERALL 43 STRATEGY 32
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
Take my opinion with a grain of salt, as I've only played this game once, but I think it's spectacular. I played it over at my friend's house pretty recently. The game is pretty quick to teach and easy to learn. 

One thing I like about this game is the amount of things you can do, it's almost overwhelming. In general, it seems like it's a good idea to stick to one or two strategies to make a lot of points instead of trying to do everything. I love the Djinns. A lot of them give bonus points for certain things so acquiring one gives you a clearer strategy to stick with.

I love moving the pieces around, I love the colors, I love the modular "board", I love the fact that you play both the action on the tile you land on and the action of the color you landed with, it adds quite a bit of strategy to the game despite it being, overall, a pretty simple game.

Then of course money converts directly to victory points at the end of the game, but you have to bid for turn order. That was the most difficult part for me in multiple ways. First, I like being the first player even if it means taking disadvantages, but it's hard to realize how much of a disadvantage you're putting yourself in if you're paying 12 gold to be first. You start with 50 gold, and when you look at all of the coins in front of you it seems like a lot, but you will quickly deplete your resources if you want to be first every turn. Second, if I bid for first, when the turn ends, the next bidding phase will be done in that order. That can be problematic because if you want to be first, or at least not last, you'll want to bid something, but since you don't know what everyone else is willing to bid you may bid 3 coins and still end up being last just because everyone else decided to bid higher. Yes, they still used more coins, but you used more coins when you bid for first last turn, and you really need to make up for your loss of money and spending money just to end up being last is a pretty big hit.

Overall, it's a great game. Well worth the praise it gets and I could see this being fun for children and adults alike.

RATING: 5
Unfortunately this is not a game for me, and it has several reason why...
1) The whole game is too artificial for my tastes. If a recall correctly the same was my problem with Goa: the whole mancala mechanic in the game is very detached from the game, even considering the minimalist approach regarding the theme in general in this game. 
2) This is a game about tactics not about strategy, short term vs. long term, reactive vs. proactive. Usually I don't like these kind of games...
3) I'm AP prone, but my girlfriend is even worse hell it can cause some serious downtime. Usually I hate people playing / texting / etc. with their phone during their games, but this was the one special case, when I did exactly this. 
Until now unfortunately Bruno Cathala is a big miss for me. He has a lot of fans out there (this game, too), but for me, there aren't enough layers to enjoy his games. Even the wonderful production quality cannot stop me from putting this to the trade pile.

RATING: 9.68
Simple and intuitive rules.....the rule book and player aids are great....very clear.   Easy to teach and first game is fun.  AP wasn't too bad for us....a little prodding was required.  I did most of the prodding, but had to be prodded myself on the last move of our first game!  :)

Several viable strategies....more tactics than strategy, but the strategy you pursue definitely influences your decisions.

It's fun to have both a tribe action and a tile action on each turn.  It's also fun to take an assassin action that let's you claim two tiles on the same turn.

The djinn powers may be the funnest (yes, that is a word) part of the game.  All-in-all it's a great game!

Link to my written review: https://www.boardgamegeek.com/thread/1379442/review-five-tribes

RATING: 7
Five Tribes is an excellent puzzle. Unfortunately, it brings out the worst in analysis paralysis prone players. God, can this game be unbearable with three other players lost deep in an ocean of combinatorics. This game is not for those people. This game requires the "ah, this move looks about right, let me do that" mentality. Otherwise this game can be a frustrating brain-burner, rather than the colorful and evolving puzzle it's intended to be.

A final complaint from me: Five Tribes isn't focused enough. It's kind of a hodge-podge of popular mechanics, from set collection, special player powers, etc. It's a little too much and takes away from the potential elegance of the game. This one's a decent 7 for me, I'll be playing this again sometime soon.

P.S. Forgot to mention that I think bidding for player order works beautifully in this game.

RATING: 8

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
29-03-2015. Rated after 1 play. FINALLY played the game of last year, Five Tribes. Five Tribes features a random setup of 4 times 5 tiles in a grid. During setup, on each tile random 3 meeples are placed. Turn structure is easy. Before all players start their turns, there is a bidding phase that determines the player order. Then, starting with the start player all players take their subsequent turns. 

At the start of your turn, you take the meeples from a tile and move orthogonally over the tiles, dropping a meeple on each tile you pass over. When you drop your last meeple, you take all the meeples in the color of the meeple you placed last and take the action of the color and strength (number of meeples). Yellow and white meeples are worth VPs at the end of the game, blue meeples allow you to score VPs for adjacent blue tiles, green allows you to get some market cards and red allows you to kill meeples on tiles. In addition, you get the action that is on the tile, which is can be to add Palm tree and Gate meeples that are worth extra VPs, allow you to buy more cards from the market, buy Djinn cards (special abilities and end-game scoring) etc. IF you take out the last meeple on a tile, you score that tile, place a camel in your color on it and get points. 

I like Five Tribes. I think it's a good game although I suspect it would be better played with less than 4 players. Now, the game felt hard to control, as each time your turn came around the whole board would have changed, so your planning meaningless. That makes the game basically a solitaire puzzle. I think it's a good puzzle, interesting choices etc. But a puzzle.. and that's a bit disappointing.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 9.1
A game where players gather meeples of different colors which give you the ability to perform five different kinds of actions. A deck of Djinn cards allows extra bonuses for certain kinds of actions. I highly recommend this game to everyone as it is superb. It has smooth mechanics and excellent components. The key to the game is how you move and gather the meeples. I think it's kind of chess-like since the way you leave the meeples on the board at the end of your turn either helps or hinders both you and your opponents. Very enjoyable and one of our favorites.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
Five Tribes is a beautifully produced game of many options to score points.  In this game players take turns picking up pieces on certain tiles and dropping them on a trail of tiles with the last tile determining your action you can take for that turn. Their are many different ways to score points in the game. The game is fairly short only lasting about 45 minutes to an hour, but is full of deep decision making.

Pros: Beautiful game production ,unique game play mechanisms(Mancala) So many strategies to gain points, fun bidding mechanism. Fast Gameplay. Little downtime

Cons: Overload of choices make it a nightmare to play with AP Players especially when the board changes every turn. The game does not allow for mistakes or taking back turns.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 10
Look, you don't have to tell me this game's faults. It's entirely abstract and is prone to slow-down when people insist on analyzing every move. But the fact remains that Five Tribes is probably my favorite pure eurogame from the last five years or so. The use of the mancala mechanic provides a familiar framework where players are free to do what they like and pursue the path they want, without being locked into a specific path. Besides that, the tactical gameplay can gives the game a nice conversational tone, assuming people aren't trying to math out every permutation on their turn. This is a 10 for me, not because it is a pristine design, but because I almost always want to play it. Not many games have captured me that way, particularly not in this genre.

RATING: 4
meh. I'm not a fan of games where the main point of the design seems to be to obscure the relative worth of different moves, in order to make the game more accessible, presumably? Like Castles of Burgandy, you just get points no matter what you do, and are prevented from calculating very much what the possible ramifications of that move are...so why am I playing? is the thought that comes to mind.  It's especially random at high player counts. Mancala, the mechanic the game was based around, is far cleverer than this. This is exactly the kind of wishy-washy paste flavoured euro that seems to get love on BGG, and exactly the kind of experience I dislike. I come from abstract games stock, want a lot of control and a lot of ability to see what every move means for future turns. If this means that new players get crushed by more experienced players, so much the better. Actually if the theme is really fun I can get into a lot of chaos and dice rolling and random events sometimes, but this theme is paper thin, a throwaway. Pass.

RATING: 5
Abstract, tactical, points grabbing nonsense. Each turn begins with the same near meaningless auction for turn order. Except for the final turn (where it's somewhat important), the top bid usually means that in amongst the Pollack-esk splash of colour the player has seen a way to earn a decent score. Those behind will see new opportunities open up as the meeple distribution changes. With the Djinn cards there's probably about ten different ways to score points just on your turn. Round and round you go until you get to counting your final score. There's no sense of story arch. It's over in about 30 minutes and it's okay. Ho hum. Players will find that along the way they've accidentally been gifted wonderful points opportunities. Oh dear. They'll find that such and such a Djinn special ability card was quite profitable because it was bought early. Yawn. It all feels so meaningless. Spot the best points grab on your turn and execute. Rinse and repeat. It's a pleasant social activity, but one where I enjoy chatting while setting up and packing away the game as much as I do playing it. Many will enjoy it much more than I do (and that's fine by me), but I'm burnt out on these types of abstract games.

RATING: 8
I think Five Tribes will be a game I enjoy every time I play. There is a lot going on, and it all revolves around my current favorite game element: the mancala (which is actually kind of a more-complicated rondel, but a very fun puzzle to work with). Where do I want to end up? And where do I need to start? And where do I want to drop off pieces along the way?

Then there are lots of great little actions along the way, be it the colors of the meeples or the tile ability or the genie cards or the resource card collection. Everything fits together quite nicely.

Despite the fact that the little about the 2-player version of this game that I know isn't ideal, I have a feeling I will get this someday. Although I mostly play 2 players with my wife, it's quite good and a game that I think would fit well into my collection some day.

RATING: 9
Got to play this at Lobster Trap 17 and even though it wasn't something I was going out of my way to learn, I happened to fall into a game.  I really like the fluid, tactical nature of the game.  The fact that money/points are linked is cool as well.  You cannot sit there and plan out your whole move because the entire state of the board could be different by the time it's your turn again.  This brings up the worst part of the game.  If you play with even one person with AP, this is going to take an eternity to play.  Trying to maximize every move is virtually impossible since there are so many moves possible in a given turn.  Not sure if the bid track is all that well done either.  I really liked it though.

RATING: 8
Received in trade (2-24-16)

Finally got this to the table and it didn't disappoint.  It's pretty easy to set-up and teach, although the scoring is a little opaque for new players until a couple of turns have gone by.  Beautiful components, great decisions, and several viable "strategic" paths to take make this a winner.  The satisfaction of landing a big move that nets you a bunch of points is palpable.

I don't find the game as "chaotic" as some have described.  You can definitely push a particular strategy, and even block strategies that your opponents are attempting.  Like many of my favorite games, there are "layers" of mastery in Five Tribes.  You can do pretty well once you learn the basic concepts, but if you really want to think things out, you can try to set up big moves for yourself on the next turn (assuming you'll win the turn order bid), you can pull off great combo's with Djinn, etc.  

Biggest "problem" with the game:  it is quite thinky.  Yes, this can lead to a little AP, but perhaps worse, there's not much casual table-conversation going on.  People are pretty focused on the board, trying to see good moves.  This elevates it from light to medium weight, in my book and means that it doesn't quite have the fun-factor that other light-medium games in my collection have (e.g., Lords of Waterdeep, Evolution).  This is not a game I would pull out on every occasion, and you have to be aware of the effect it will have on your table.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
I have played this a few times which is good going for me with new games. A good game that is quite easy to teach but has strategy and tactical play that is challenging enough to engage the gamer in me. It has a certain blocking quality, slightly higher than Carcassonne, which provides the interaction ie take something that you know others want even if it is suboptimal for your score. Just a solid game that has a basic gameplay (similar to mancala I am told) mechanism not used in other games I own(picking up meeples and placing them in a trail on the tiles and actioning the last tile you place a meeple on).

RATING: 7
[2014-10-25]
A Mancala szisztéma, annyit lépünk, ahányan egy lapkán vagyunk mechanizmus nagyon jó kis csavarral került bele ebbe a játékba. Négy különböző színű és tudású meeple-ből random 3 kerül fel minden lapkára és miközben haladunk velük egyet-egyet elszórunk az útközben érintett lapkára. Ahova megérkezünk egy színben minden meeplet felhasználunk a tudásuk szerint. A játékban rengetegféle győzelmi pont szerzési lehetőség van, minden játék más. Érdekes, színes, egyetlen baja van, hogy rettentő AP veszélyes. Kicsit a Mangrovia alá lőném, de jár ennek is a 7 pont elsőre. Tetszett!

RATING: 9
Five Tribes is a unique offering from Days of Wonder as it is a bit above the weight of the games they are typically known for. However, it's quite straightforward in how it plays and that is what I love most about it. I've heard the criticism that it is prone to extremely long periods of downtime while someone is thinking of their move and that is a valid complaint. However, I try to get people to not care so much about making the best move and simply going for the first good move they see. In that way it speeds up the game and the scores are usually very tight. It's an elegant design that is matched by DoW's usual production standard. Definitely top ten material for me.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 5
I am not a fan of some of the design decisions that went into Five Tribes. I'll start with the djinn cards. They won't add replayability for me because I won't play the game more than a few times. Instead they create rules overhead, randomness, and they require players to track which meeples go back into the bag and which ones don't, which is tedious. Next, there's the set collection cards. They feel less tacked on than the djinn cards but they make it more difficult to discern who's winning without slowing down what can be an already slow game. They could have been excised. There is an unhealthy tradeoff in Five Tribes between playing quickly and playing optimally, and the tradeoff is exacerbated by the inclusion of so many ways to score points. I suspect that they were added to increase replayability but, for me anyway, they will do the opposite.

There are pieces of the game I like. Bidding for turn order with VPs is a good idea. And there is a healthy amount of interaction and cross-incentivization on the board. But at the end of the day, I think the design could have used an editor. Five Tribes might have been a good small-box 2p game.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 8
The actual gameplay of this game is fairly easy to teach.  There is a layout of tiles with different colored meeples strewn about.  You pick up all the meeples from one tile and move them across other tiles.  When you drop a meeple onto your final tile in your move you collect all the meeples from that tile of that color and perform the meeple and tile's special action.

However, actually playing the game is a lot to grok.  I, myself, did very poorly in my first play because I just didn't decipher the board very well.  I was missing moves that would have been better options for myself while completely setting up my opponents who went after me.  That's really what this game is about too, managing your moves so that you get the most benefit from them while providing as little as possible to the other players.  You have to be really careful with this though because there are a lot of players who will slow this game to a standstill trying to discover the perfect move on each turn.  While I played poorly I can at least say I didn't hold up the game with AP.

I think it is an incredibly clever game, despite it's cutesy exterior and bright colors it kind of surprised me with its depth and complexity and I play a lot of very heavy games.  It's probably mostly because I just wouldn't normally expect a game of this weight from Days of Wonder or Bruno Cathala.  In the end I enjoyed it and it is definitely something I want to play more and challenge myself to do better at.

It is also definitely worth mentioning that the components are of mostly the highest quality.  My victory point chits weren't punched the best and were a little hard to punch out without tearing and getting a little extra paper attached.  But everything else is sturdy and beautiful.  Be warned though there is giong to be a lot of pastel colored wood out on the play area at once which can make it kind of hard to decipher a camel from a meeple, from a palace, etc.  The method we used to help with this is to stand up all the meeples but lay camels, palm trees, and palaces flat.

After several plays this game has elevated itself in my eyes even more.  The main reason for that being I think you can legitimately follow any one of several different strategies and win this game.  That's always a great boon when a game like this has so many different scoring opportunities and all of them are viable.  I've seen clever use of the assassins, collecting huge lots of resources, taking advantage of powerful djinn powers, and lining up rows and rows of viziers and elders all be winning or at least very close to winning strategies as well as others and hybrids thereof.  Such a well designed and smoothly running game.

RATING: 5
Played 3 times in one afternoon.  I like (part of) this design - it presents a puzzle that rewards creative thinking. However, it can reduce 'gamerz' to absolute paralysis, weighing the auction , then every move thereafter. 

Despite the Djinns (who give some direction) it is a tactical game, and this does not suit some.

It needs to be played at speed, weighing up and deciding on the best 3 paths before your turn. However you will then have to recalculate when it gets to them because the board will have changed.

The board, meeple, Djinn setup and the variable game end conditions meant each of my three games played out very differently, with the different parts of the points salad taking centre plate in each game. There was some interaction, competition for yellow meeples and the desire to not leave juicy control pickings kept participants as much interested in fellow players as their own path.

After 3 plays in one day have not had the inclination to play again. Mainly because I won't play it with any one who suffers from AP, or anyone who might suffer from AP. And also because I find balance between tactics and strategy is too heavily weighted towards the tactical for the length of play.

An interesting game for sure, but it has a bit of a problem with analysis--it is easy to make your move as the first player, because the board remains intact during the re-stocking phase and your plan is simple enough to execute.  The problem lies in that board changes, often significantly, as each player takes their move.  If you're not first player, you will often have to re-evaluate your turn from the start.  In one sense that tends to keep players engaged during the other player's turn, if you're next.  But if you're the fourth player, you might as well get up and go for a walk while the other players take their turns, because everything will change by the time it gets to you.

Point salad?  No, not really, just multiple areas to focus on for many different paths to victory.  It's great in that respect.

Overall arc and timing of the game--excellent.

Balance--decent.  The djinn are not well balanced at all, with several being obviously more valuable than others.  But since Djinn are not necessary to win, it's not a huge issue.  There should be a way to clear a stagnant face up pool though.

Artwork--decent.  Lots of wood.  Like all these annoyingly movable lay out the tiles to form the play area games, it really should have a board with recessed areas for the tiles to fit in.  It would also help to have 9 marked areas for the resource cards since 9 is not a visually automatic identifying number....you often have to count out the cards remaining.

RATING: 8.5
Great design with a perfect balance between meaty gameplay and simple mechanics. A innovative twist of the mancala mechanic, surprisingly heavy for a DOW title. I really enjoy planning the best move with limited resources according to game state.

That being said the game is not without problems. AP hell with the wrong players. Scoring is obfuscated (if you need a pad to calculate scoring, something is wrong). Weaker player can give the game to following players. Djinn can swing the game like creatures in Cyclades.

Still a very good experience for me, I would very much like to play again.

RATING: 6
Family game on a sort of grid mancala. Pick up all the meeples on a tile, then distribute them along a set of orthogonally adjacent tiles. Collect meeples on the space you end at, taking some action depending on the colour, and also an action for the type of tile.

Scoring is a point salad with some eight different sources of VP.

There are significant changes between turns, so play is almost entirely tactical; worse, there's enough to think about that there is painful potential for analysis paralysis.

It's entertaining enough in play but a disappointing total package.

RATING: 7.2
Oh the hype. I don't usually buy in to it, but that doesn't mean I don't want to play the games of course.
The last couple of years (like say, since [thing=27746][/thing]) Days of Wonder hadn't put out games that interested me a lot. And this game looked to be more of that, but then they started saying how this would be one of their heaviest games. One of? Probably, but not the heaviest, I think [thing=27746][/thing] is probably still their heaviest. But this game is not a superfiller. Bruno Cathala has done some very meh games and some quite good games like [thing=21763][/thing], so that didn't say much for me.
The theme for this game is completely pasted on so let's not bother with that. It has a fun mechanism where you pick up a group of meeples and drop off one on each tile you pass over and the last tile is triggered with the color you dropped there. This might lead to AP as there are a lot of options, especially early on, but the best options should be quite obvious. All you have to do is figure out which of those is the best for your strategy. And the way the scoring is constructed does open up the game so multiple different strategies can be explored. In our 4 player game everyone was doing something different. But that didn't mean we weren't doing things that didn't annoy the other players. There will always be overlap between players.
I must say that this is a very solid design indeed. Hype worthy? Maybe not, but certainly worth a good deal of attention.

Initial rating: 7.2/10

RATING: 10
Amazing!
There is much going on in this game: The unusual "Mancala" mechanic, modular board, collecting trading goods, Djinns act as special powers, auction for turn order, area control, people effects, area effects and much more. And the different mechanics interact with each other. Different strategies emerge depending on your choices and the options that present themselves to you in a given game.
The Arabian style fantasy theme is nice, materials and artworks are great.
It's an awesome game with a good level of complexity.
Yes, it can be tough to play because it is very hard to see and weight all possible moves (especially on the full board at the beginning of the game). You have to like that challenge and to decide more through instinct than exact calculation.

in short:
+ theme
++ mechanics
+ modular board
+ great artworks (especially the Djinns)
+ materials
- expensive
- can be hard to play ?

RATING: N/A
Jól összerakott és gyönyőrűszépen kivitelezett játék. A játék alapaja egy négyzetrács mezőire adaptált Mancala-mechanizmus.

A négyzetrács mezői tele vannak rakva különböző színű bábúkkal és amikor valakire sor kerül, felveszi az összes bábút egy mezőről és addig lépked élszomszédosan - egy-egy bábút mindenhol hátrahagyva - amíg el nem fogynak a bábúk. Azon a mezőn aztán, ahova az utolsó bábút tette, két akciót is végrehajt. Az egyik akció attól függ, hogy milyen színű volt a bábú, amit oda rakott, a másik pedig attól, hogy melyik mezőn állt meg.

Minden színhez más akció tartozik és persze a mezők is mind különböző tipusú akciókat adhatnak. De vannak megszerezhető dzsin lapkák is, amelyek mind különböző folyamatos előnyt adnak annak a játékosnak, aki megszerezte.

Mivel fontos a játékossorrend, minden kör elején licitálunk a játékos sorrendre. De ez csak egy egykörös licit, ami nem húzza el a játékidőt, csak keménnyebbé teszi a taktikai döntéseket, hisz a pénz egy-az-egyben váltódik győzelmi pontra a játék végén.

A játék folyamán fokozatosan fogynak a bábúk a térképről, mígnem a végén már nem lehet lépni. A tapasztalat az, hogy az elején bár több a lehetőség, a játékosok nem számolnak végig minden lehetőséget, így viszonylag gyorsan lépnek. A játék vége felé viszont már egyre kevesebb a lehetőség, de mivel mindenki végig számloja az összes lehetőséget, lassabban lépnek. 

A mechanizmusok elég változatosak ahhoz, hogy mindenki megtalálja a maga útját a legtöbb pont megszerzéséhez.

RATING: 9
I'm usually hesitant to give a "9" to a game I've played only once, but I really, really enjoyed Five Tribes.  It isn't just eye candy, it really does have a lot of things going on.  That said, I didn't find myself in "analysis paralysis" like some people say the game induces. RATING: 8
Wow, finding the Mancala-style combinations of meeples in this game is incredibly satisfying! This is one of those games that brings the best of Euro-style and Ameritrash games together.  Euro games are praised for their excellent game mechanics and satisfying points scoring, but often lack in rich, aesthetically pleasing experiences. Conversely, Ameritrash games are characterized as being highly themeatic and flashy, but a bit shallow on gameplay.  I think Five Tribes brings the best of both worlds together in a glorious and rich gaming experience, without compromising in any category.  A beautiful and satisfying game.

RATING: 10
I'm so enamored by this game! I can't stop thinking about it. I got to learn the game at Gen Con, me and my friend waited by Bruno's table so we could learn from him and both him and the game were a blast. Who knew how hilarious Bruno was? Me and my friend waited by the doors three hours before they opened the next day to rush in and be one of the first 50 to get the game and get it signed, another treat! Though I felt bad getting it signed in front of a line of people that was just behind us and was getting told the game sold out... we should have maybe waited to get it signed haha. 

Anyways I have played the game as often as I could since I got back home and I love it so much. There is so many interesting things to do every turn and it feels so satisfying to make big plays. The rules are very easy to teach and it seems that right away people are making strategic and clever plays. I thought the game could have some AP problems but have never found any issue with the game taking any longer than around an hour. The bidding at the beginning of each turn is so interesting as this is really where the champion of the game is made. Every bit of design feels so clever and tied in with every other element, like the cards not being replaced immediately as they are purchases. This mirrors the rest of the game as decisions diminish each round. This ties back into the bid for turn order being so important. If you want to do something that turn bid high, as it may be gone before you get to it. But getting to something immediately doesn't make the game for you either, if you see no good move, or you got outbid, the layout is ever changing as every player before you manipulates the board and cards until your turn and a great turn can arise out of that. Its a very tactical game and with the Djinns and some of the scoring elements, especially with the sets of cards and yellow meeples, it is a strategic game too. 

I really can't say enough good things about the game. With more plays I may move the game to a ten even, something I don't do lightly! Bruno, if you read this I want to think you personally for the great opportunity to learn this game from you and for the hours of wonderful fun the game provides me and my friends. 
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
3/17/2017: I have played this game several times and this has become one of my wife's favorite games from Bruno Cathala. This game is essentially a point salad game that allows you to gain points from various ways. 

The interesting element of the game is the implementation of Mancala as the main mechanic. With the combination of Mancala mechanic and point salad element, it allows players to pursue different strategy in the game. 

Additionally, the auction mechanic for the turn order also allows people to either execute the action that they want or block someone from their obvious move. 

This is a very easy game to teach and hard to master. I am always willing to bring this game to the table! :)

RATING: 6.5
I can see the appeal of Five Tribes.  The game is accessible and has nice components.  There are a lot of decisions to be made and the board is constantly changing.  But there is virtually no long term strategy.  It is purely tactical to just optimize your current move.  At least in 4 player, it's nearly impossible to think more than one turn ahead. Djinns can be really powerful while Viziers and Assassins are very underwhelming.  It's a fine game, but there's nothing that would make me want to pick it from a pile of other games.

RATING: 8.5
Before playing, Five Tribes both intrigued me and scared me, as I had previously had bad experiences with the mancala mechanism in Trajan. While this seems more complex as it is implemented here, and essentially the entirety of the game, I think it is less punishing.

I find Five Tribes to be a beautiful puzzle, as you try to work out how to get even a decent action without setting up your opponents. The bidding system was initially a headache but it's growing on me, and really takes a few plays to know what a turn's worth is and the acceptable margin between that and your bid. I just love the way they have implemented the mancala mechanism, and how freaking fun it is.

RATING: 8
Lots to think about (but not overwhelming) and great wooden bits. Having a bid for turn order is usually a sign of lazy game design but the way it works here is really well done. You have an auction track (kind of similar to Homesteaders or Speicherstadt) and each person just gets one bid on the track. Bid too little and you might miss out on that big combo you were planning, but bid too much and you could end up losing more points than you gain on that turn.

There's a decent amount of luck but probably no more than most middle-weight euros. If the perfect Djinn comes out at the right time, or the right cards come out of the deck, a player can rack up on points. Rating could go up or down based on how this affects future games.

Great game.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 9
This game has really grown on me. The bidding for turn order mechanic is genius and pulling off a cool/unorthodox move that works out is very satisfying. 2 player game is quite different than the 3-4 player versions (you can have consecutive moves quite easily with 2 players, in the 3-4 player game you have to engineer that situation far more carefully) but both have their charms. The game components are all excellent as well (camels + palm tress + palaces!) My biggest complaint is that I wish you couldn't kill other players' meeples with assassin, it's not like they're doing anything once a player gets them so it has no strategic effect to it, it's just mean.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
I love this game.  I LOOOOOOVE this game.  At no point will I ever be able to say enough about how much I love this game.  But since that's not helpful, here goes:

I love Days of Wonder games and I love games designed by Bruno Cathala.  So far when the two meet, it's always a win.

The theme is exciting, set in the aesthetic of Scheherazade and filled with genies, intrigue, etc.  Yes, most of it is abstract, but use you g*& d$%^ imagination.  "Pasted-on theme" is just such a dumb term anyway.

The art is spectacular.  I just want to look at this game and it's components all day long.  Each djinn card is gorgeous, and, I mean, camel meeples!  CAMEL MEEPLES!!!

Most importantly, this game is mechanically one of the most fun and engaging games I've ever played.  It's essentially a beefed up mancala, wherein you pick up all meeples from a tile and deposit them one at a time until ending on a tile with at least one meeple of the same color, activating the ability of that color.  Each ending move creates new abilities and options.  The variety of possibilities each turn is dramatic. (note: if you don't like playing games that could produce AP, or playing with people who have it, this isn't one for you).

I find this game endlessly replayable thanks to the choice to make the main play space a grid of tiles rather than a static board.  I have never had the same strategy twice, and I enjoy a game in which my strategy at the outset implodes by turn three and I have to adjust.  It's possible to disengage when it's not your turn, but you will be 100% lost when your attention returns to the table.  So it commands your attention.  And I LOVE a game that keeps everyone's eyes on the table rather than their phones.

**final note**  I purchased this game AFTER Days of Wonder switched the slave commodity cards into fakir cards.  I did not want any game on my shelf in which a primary path to victory was human trafficking.  I am SO glad they made this change.  Now instead of buying slaves, I am recruiting fakirs (Islamic mendicants) in hopes of gaining powerful genies on my side.  The djinns produce major effects, so you simply cannot do without them.  And now I can play this game and never have that moment of terrible discomfort as a game set in a hyper-fantasized story realm broods with an unnecessary dose of historical grotesqueness.  Kudos to Days of Wonder for fixing what, I think, was just a total bonehead oversight on the part of the publisher (a great one) and designer (one of my favorites).
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 9
Fantastic game with so many layers of strategy, and so many differing paths to victory. I love the fact that you can change your strategy throughout the game depending on both the board & what other players are focusing on.  A few of the Djins are underpowered in 2 player games, with their powers being focused more on the multiplayer element, but I usually remove them if not playing with 3 or 4 (after a few games occurred when all 3 of the Djinns available were of this ilk) . The production values and artwork are beautiful. This game is not for everyone. You can suffer from AP as you have so many options & are worried about what you will leave for everyone else. But its biggest drawback to some, is its greatest asset to others & if you and your group are ok with that their is so much to like here.

RATING: 7
A perfectly pleasurable game but it isn't great.  The decision space is mathematically enormous at the outset which definitely adds some interesting decision making.  

The play time is nice at about 45 minutes unless you have someone with extreme AP.  The rules are shockingly short but at the same time slightly lacking in clarify, that said it is a simple game to understand.

Ultimately it's a point salad abstract game with some take that elements and some interesting decisions to be made.

The components are excellent as well.

Like I said before a good but not great game.

I like the additions of the Artisans expansion and it feels like it almost "completes" the game.

RATING: 3
Only game I've played where AP affected me more than anyone else at the table. Too many options at the beginning with no clear way to find the optimal move. Or strategy. Once you start to identify a plan or strategy, the number of options have diminished and you find yourself scrambling to choose a strategy from a set of completely uncompelling strategies. Toward the endgame, when you want to stay in contact with competitors, the options to put your strategy forward are few and far between. 

What makes me resent the game is how it entices you to develop a strategy, only to completely change the foundation of that strategy multiplied by the number of people going before you. It is impossible to efficiently plan ahead, and we have the game's system to blame rather than our fellow players. Absolutely hate how you can have a plan and then have to start over at the beginning of your turn instead of making your play. A LOT of downtime between turns in a 4 player game since all can really do is wait and/or read the list of  overwhelming djinn powers for the umpteenth time. 

The least fun I've had playing a board game in a LONG time. The continual dread of agricola is more bearable because at least in that game you have little sheep to push around and the other farmers aren't dropping crap in the middle of your field and stealing all your vegetables.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
Five tribes is a game played on a random arrangement of tiles which start with three random meeples. On a round, you bid for turn order, and then resolve your turn. Your turn consists of picking a tile, taking all the meeples on that tile and allocating the meeples, one at a time mancala style, adjacent first to your start tile, but thereafter adjacent to the last tile you put a meeple on. You can't go diagonally, you can't immediately return to a tile you just left, and you have to end by placing your last meeple on a tile that has the same color meeple on it. You trigger the action on the last tile you land on. And you trigger the color meeple you used on the last tile, removing all meeples of that color on that tile. Different colored meeples do different special things. If you ever remove all the meeples off a tile, you claim it for your own, and you get the points of the tile at game end. There's also a set collection market thing going on. And you can buy special action cards that do different things and score points at game end.

The game is largely tactical, where you are looking for the best move every turn. What other people do on their turn could potentially screw your options, or open up new options. The auction for turn order is based off a fixed track, where you bid 0 , 1, 3, 5, 8, 12 or 18, and with the exception of 0, only a single player can take a spot. If you pay a lot to get first crack at the board this turn, you're likely not to go very early in the next turn.

The game ends when a player runs out of markers to claim tiles, or when there are no legal movements anymore. The game gets really interesting as it nears the end of the game, as options become terribly limited. What to bid for turn order becomes very important, as few moves are likely to justify bidding much, but the first move will likely return a lot more than any other move.

I liked this game a lot. There's a lot to take into account, and it challenges you to think on your turn, but it's pretty quick. We didn't really run into AP issues on our play, but it's a game where that could be a problem.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: N/A
2-4;2-4;none
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7.5
[thing=157354][/thing] is a well knitted game, smart, and with some great art and sturdy components. However, the game sure didn't grabbed my attention - it is fine, don't get me wrong, but it is so detached from its theme, that I didn't felt it at all, not even a little bit. This is so noticeable because I do enjoy so much a good theme, and Arabian themed games usually have a mystic veil on them that attracts me, specially when you link a game to the world of [i]1001 Nights[/i]. By the way, I haven't felt a game so off its pretended since [thing=102680][/thing], and it is curious to me that both these games share the central mechanic of the mancala.
	
Now that I talked about it, the mancala works beautifully in the game, and it is the major source of fun taking the meeples, dropping them on the way, until you reach your desireable spot to take the meeples that of the same color and activating the type of meeples and the tile they were on. This is basically the whole game. Except that, at first, you bid for turn order. It is not really an auction - there is a track, in some of the spaces there is a cost, which you pay if you want to put your marker there. If someone wants to pay more to put in a space in front of you, they can, and there is nothing you can do (you can't pay more). If you put your marker on the 0 (zero) spot, you put it in the first space, but you can be pushed behind if others put their markers also on the zero spot but did it after you. Once this is decided, following the round order, players take meeples from a tile, leaving meeples along the way, until you reach the place you want. You activate the meeples of the same color of the one you hold for last - you take them from the tile, no matter which color they are, and some go to the box, others (yellow/vizirs and white/elders) you keep for yourself, for later use. If you take the last meeples of a tile, you take the tile for you, placing a camel of your color on it (tiles are worth points, and also, in some of them you can also claim (if they are already there), or put, palm trees and castles - which are also worth points in the end game).

The game ends when someone is out os camels or there is no more legal moves to be made with the meeples left in the board. The player with the most points wins!

I thought I would feel the downgrade of options on the game as time passes way more than I did. But I was fine with it - sure, there are less options as the game advances, since the board will have less and less meeples, but then usually the game changes a little bit from activating meeples to claiming tiles (and putting palm trees and castles). And, even when the options were really slim, the game end was just a few minutes away.

The interaction in the game is mostly indirect - taking what someone else maybe wanting before them. The assassins (red) can kill vizirs (yellow) and elders (white) from other players, and this can mean a good deal of points, specially with the vizirs (since they score accordingly to how many players have less than you). There is some tension for the round order bid, but, usually, everyone will do their thing - but, if you play before someone, and you have a good guess of what he/she will be wanting, specially regarding taking hold of tiles, you can prevent him/her by leaving a meeple in a tile that had a single meeple - this can be really useful to put a break in their intentions. But, regarding activation of tiles, there is little to be done.

Anyway, [thing=157354][/thing] plays reasonable fast (around 40-50 minutes) and allows for a fine amount of decision-making for such a short span of time. I also found that playing the game is even a bit relaxing, probably because I enjoy to grab meeples like I'm an angry Arabian djinn! But the lack of real theme make [thing=157354][/thing], overall, just a little above-average game, and it could be a great one.

Finally, there are two things I just could not stop seeing when playing, no matter how hard I tried:
[ImageID=2296425]

RATING: 7
Medium - Abstract - 7.0 Includes - Artisans of Naqala
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
Five Tribes borrows heavily from the mancala mechanism of taking all the meeples on one tile and dropping them on a series of adjacent tiles. Bonuses are conveyed both by the type of meeples dropped, actions carried out on final tile, claiming of empty tiles and also set collection of goods. There are at least 8-10 categories for scoring and many folks have pointed out that this ends up being a Feldian point salad game. There is some truth to these claims. Virtually any action you take should yield some form of points directly or indirectly. However, some paths do force you to specialize and invest several turns in order to accrue points. Set collection of goods in particular requires that you grab different types of goods in order to hit the higher returns. However, most point salad scoring games also reward some form of specialization.

What is refreshing about Five Tribes isn't necessarily the scoring but the mancala mechanism. One wonders if the game would be better serve being more Knizian than Feldian. Stripping the scoring to the basic essentials, perhaps even just scoring colored meeples or majorities ala Samurai, might make the game shorter and more streamlined while keeping the core experience of the game. In a 2 player setting, the game is quicker in pace in the early stages, but starts to drag toward the end as options are limited. Instead of dropping meeple on tiles I want, I end up playing defensively to avoid opening up opportunities for my opponent to claim tiles. This really bogs down game play and increases play time unnecessarily.

That said, the game is good if not great. The mancala mechanism is fun and being able to look at the board in totality to find "paths' can be rewarding. I just wish the scoring is streamlined to extract the main essence of playing Five Tribes.

RATING: 2
I'm not fond of games like this anymore. I can definitely understand the appeal; the design is simple and straight to the point. 

It just feels like a giant puzzle where all the players are trying to solve it optimally. There's always a best move and the decision space is simply not one that interests me anymore. All these annoyances just lead to such an uninspiring game. It works, there's nothing broken about it, but it left me with a feeling of emptiness. I just don't care for it.

Original, Feb 2017:
Why did I wait so long to buy this one?!  I LOVE this game.  It seems simple, but is deceptively so.

The rules took a little longer to explain to my parents, but I think by halfway through the game they started (mostly) understanding the point of it.  I wouldn't consider this a first or second gateway game, but could be one to introduce to someone who has a few games under their belt.  I'd say it's a mid-weight game, just slightly to the lighter side.

Why I love it:
* There are multiple ways to achieve victory.  This means your initial strategy may be foiled, but you can adapt to a new strategy.  You will be putting some thought into every move you make, especially as you get farther into the game.
* There are lots of parts and pieces to this game.  It's overwhelming at first glance but all pulls together surprisingly well to make a rich, complex game.  I think the Djinns are the only part I'd say I could live without, but even they add another layer to consider.  (If you're playing with new gamers and want to make it lighter, you could definitely play without them.)
* Theme fits pretty well.  It's not super immersive, but I can see how every part and piece works together to strengthen the theme.  Each meeple has a fitting purpose, and camels, oasis, and palaces all tie in nicely too.

It's just a great game!  I could go on and on about how much I LOVE this game, but you should try it out yourself.  If it does feel too complex, I'd definitely suggest playing without Djinns for your first game to learn the basics first.

RATING: 7
Apparently the big hit from GenCon 2014 and very highly rated on BGG.

A good, solid game but nothing spectacular and nothing to warrant the GenCon hype. Innovative, interesting, Mancala/Kalaha-like worker placement (sort of) mechanism. A bit chaotic and a bit of unavoidable downtime as your options are very likely to change significantly during others turns - would be a nightmare with AP-prone players. Completely pasted on theme.

A game I would be happy to play occasionally with the right people if nothing better was available, but not at all a game to buy or seek out.

RATING: 9.5

A lot of people say that the bidding is broken with two because you can set yourself up to take 4 turns in a row but so can your opponent (they can also block you and vice versa) and it's expensive to do and sometimes it's not even the best move (like when you want your openent to move pieces to set up turns for you at the beginning of the game) so really this point is not valid.

RATING: 6
This game seems to have triggered a strong love it/hate it response in almost everyone. I come down somewhere in the middle. I like the Trajan-like puzzle of figuring out the best mandala move each turn and I love how the decision tree actually grows smaller as the game goes on, reigning in the AP and pushing the game to conclusion. This is a great feature in a casual game.

Otherwise, I don't think it's a great game by any measurement. In fact, I'm pretty sure it will get unpleasantly repetitive in regular rotation. Yet, I think it can hold up well as an occasional distraction, as long as no one takes it to seriously. (Such as complaining about a medieval Sultan utilizing a slave market.)

RATING: 6.5
I love minimalist Euros but Five Tribes just doesn't do it for me. The mancala mechanic is fine but there's way too much that changes on any given turn to really plan anything and that leads it to be a little chaotic and difficult to follow sometimes. I guess you could argue the turn bidding should be factored in when planning plays but often times the correct play is to low bid and hope your opponent misplays. Speaking of which...

Unintentional kingmaking seems to be pretty common. One slight misstep could swing the entire game toward someone that really didn't deserve it based on that turn's order.

It's visually very pretty, and it is a fairly decent intro game but I don't get it to the table often for a reason.

RATING: 6
One day someone will create a core mechanic with arbitrarily high complexity such that not even one move ahead could possibly be planned for by anything other than a supercomputer. Gamers everywhere will argue about whether this game is pure skill or pure luck.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
Arabian Nights themed mancala strategy game. Setup random 5x6 play area of location tiles and seed each with 3 random tribe meeples (elders, viziers, merchants, builders, and assassins). Take meeples from a starting tile and move to a target location depositing one meeple at each tile along the way until reaching the destination tile where you may claim one color of its tribe members. Depending on the tribe, they can be used to summon a helping djinn or demon, get rare merchandise including slaves, earn coin, or assassinate other tribe members. Lots to think about; how much to bid for turn order, meeple movement tactics constantly change, while trying to not help the next player, also considering which location benefits to control, maxing the djinns power, and collecting a set of unique goods to sell for big coin.  I agree with other player comments that the unpredictable flux of the game makes strategy practically impossible, so don't bother figuring out your move ahead of time and just wait for your turn to eventually come around. In fact, I usually bid zero and go last and take whatever lands my way and win more often than not. Definitely can induce AP.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 6.5

RATING: 8
I was interested in Five Tribes since it was announced, but due to Tyler’s skepticism of the game, and a few other minor things, I held off on picking it up. A sale as well as hearing so many good things about this game weakened my resolve. I bought a copy and hoped that it would go over well despite Tyler’s doubts. It turned out to be a strong success. I think Tyler liked it even more than I did (and I really like it!). I played a lot of mancala as a kid and this hits the right spots to become the mancala of my adult life. It’s extremely smart, easy to learn, and we find the 2P version to suit us extremely well. Neither Tyler nor I are very AP-prone, so the game moves along within a reasonable time. Honestly, my biggest complaint is that the game is completely color dependent, so we’ll never be able to play this with our colorblind friend (he can’t tell a large number of colors apart, more than just the expected red/green and blue/purple). I fully plan to pick up Five Tribes: The Artisans of Naqala at a later date so we can see how that shakes up the game.

RATING: 8.5
I love this game. I hate playing it with people, especially multiple people. Am I the only one who doesn't suffer from terrible analysis paralysis with this game? By the way, studying the board to find the move that gives you the most points this turn ISN'T the best strategy, I promise ;) Honestly, just pick three of the tribes, rank them one to three in your personal priorities, then pursue them, as a turn, in that order. I am also going to instill a three minute timer on everyone's turn. Seriously, I can't handle waiting an hour for my turn, NO game is worth that. That is my only complaint really is the AP people seem to get. Remove that mental obstacle and this is one of the most well made strategy games out there. Perfectly combines strategy with tactics, gives the mancala mechanic a modern make over and has a feeling unlike any other game in its' weight. As always, DOW knocks this out of the park.

Artwork- 9/10

Fun- 8/10

RATING: 3

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8.1
Days of Wonder is known for the fact that they produce games with wonderful components, which are also a lot of fun to play. At least, that for the games we known from them, until they released Relic Runners last year. That game looked also great, but it wasn’t fun to play at all. Also the games Cargo Noir, Small World Realms and Small World Underground we liked less. Both of them are nice, but not games that we want to play on regular base. This made us less curious to Five Tribes. Yet, when we were able to play this one, we didn’t hesitate and joined the table. As usual for a DOW game, the game is pretty easy to explain, which made it accessible for families. The components are also just great. Nice meeples, wooden castles, wooden palmtrees and wooden camels.

On a player’s turn, he’ll pick up all of the meeples on a single tile, then he’ll drop them, one at a time, on an orthogonal path. The last meeple he drops, he must drop on a tile that already has a meeple of that color. Then he picks up all the meeples of that color and does that meeple action. Then he does the tile’s action. If there are no more meeples on the tile (and no camel already there), he places his camel there. The actions that come with the different color meeples are:

    Blue (Merchants) – Count the number of merchants you took (plus any slaves you want to turn in) and multiply that by the number of blue tiles adjacent to the current tile (also include the tile itself). You get that amount of money and the blue meeples go in the bag.
    Red (Assassins) – Count the number of assassins you took (plus any slaves you want to turn in) and trace an orthogonal path that far from the current tile. Kill (put in the bag) a single meeple on that path (including the starting tile). If you emptied a tile by doing this, put your camel on it. Alternatively, kill (put in the bag) one meeple that another player has. Then put the assassins you took in the bag.
    Green (Trader) – Count the number of traders you took (X) and take the first X market cards. Then put the traders in the bag.
    Yellow (Viziers) – Take the viziers and put them in front of you.
    White (Elders) – Take the elders and put them in front of you.

This repeats until the end of the game. At the end of the game, the player with the most points wins. A game ends, when a player places his last camel or nobody can make a legal move.

Gameplay is changed just a little bit for a 2-player game. Each player gets two turns in a round instead of one. It makes for a fun little mental sparring when bidding (now twice each round!) for turn order. Do I want to let my opponent go twice in a row?? (In 2-player it is even possible for a person to go 4 times in a row – going 3rd and 4th in one round and then 1st and 2nd in the next). 2-player games really open some neat strategic options based simply upon the ability to chain turns together.

This is again an old-style Days of Wonder game. We liked it a lot. Nice gameplay, nice components. Nothing to complain about. Of course there is. The theme is pasted on. The game has nothing to do with 1001 nights. It just gathering points everywhere you can. If you like a light Feld game, we’re pretty sure, this one is right up in your alley.
